What Is Kalam?

Vowels and Consonants: Definitions and Phonetic Concepts
Defines vowels, consonants, and the phonetic meaning of motion and quiescence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definitions of vowels (musawwata) and consonants (samita) in Arabic phonetics, and the meaning of motion (haraka) and quiescence (sukun) as phonetic qualities.

Core Idea
Arabic vowels are prolongations of short vowel gestures; consonants are all other letters.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies foundational phonetic terms used in discussions of kalam. Before analyzing arguments about utterance initiation, the author defines what vowels and consonants are, emphasizing that they are not physical properties but phonetic qualities. Understanding these definitions is essential for the subsequent discussion about beginning with a vowel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three vowel letters
Read the first sentence to find the three vowel letters (alif, waw, ya) and their origins from the short vowels fatha, damma, kasra.
These are called 'prolonged vowels'.
2
Understand the consonant category
Notice that consonants include all letters that are not vowels, including waw and ya when they are not preceded by a homologous vowel.
The alif is always a vowel.
3
Grasp the special meaning of motion
The author explains that 'motion' here is not physical movement but the inclination of a consonant's articulation towards one of the three vowel articulations.
This is a phonetic definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعني الألف من الفتحة والواو من الضمة، والياء من الكسرة تسمى المصوتة الممدودة، وهي وما سوى المصوتة تسمى صامتة، ويندرج فيها الواو والياء المتحركتان أو الساكنتان، إذ لم يكن قبل الواو ضمة وقبل الياء كسرة وأما الألف فلا يكون إلا مصوتاً، وإطلاقها على الهمزة باشتراك الاسم، وليس المراد بالحركة والسكون هاهنا ما هو من خواص الأجسام، بل الحركة عبارة عن كيفية حاصلة في الحرف الصامت من إمالة مخرجه إلى مخرج إحدى المدات، فإلى الألف فتحة وإلى الواو ضمة. وإلى الياء كسرة . ولا خلاف في امتناع الابتداء بالمصوت وإنما الخلاف في أن ذلك بسكونه حتى يمتنع الابتداء بالساكن الصامت أيضاً أو لذاته لكونه عبارة عن مدة متولدة من إشباع حركة تجانسها فلا يتصور إلا حيث يكون قبلها صامت متحرك وهذا هو الحق لأن كل سليم الحس يجد من نفسه إمكان الابتداء بالساكن، وإن كان مرفوضاً في لغة العرب كالوقف على المتحرك والجمع بين الساكنين من الصوامت إلا في الوقف مثل زيد وعمرو، أو إذا كان الصامت الأول حرف لين والثاني مدغماً نحو خويصة فإنه جائز، كما إذا كان الأول مصوتاً نحو دابة وعدم قدرة البعض على الابتداء بالساكن لا يدل على امتناعه كالتلفظ ببعض الحروف فإن ذلك لقصور في الآلة والاستدلال على الإمكان بأن المصوت أينما كان مشروط بالصامت، فلو كان الصامت مشروطاً به في بعض المواضع كالابتداء لزم الدور، وليس بشيء لأن المصوت مشروط بأن يسبقه صامت والصامت في الابتداء مشروط بأن يلحقه مصوت فيكونان معاً ولا إستحالة فيه.
I mean: the alif from the fatha, the waw from the damma, and the ya from the kasra are called the prolonged vowels (al-musawwata al-mamduda). And these and what is other than the vowel are called consonants (samita). Included among them are the waw and ya that are either vocalized or quiescent, when there is not before the waw a damma nor before the ya a kasra. As for the alif, it is only a vowel. Their application to the hamza is by homonymy. What is meant here by motion (haraka) and quiescence (sukun) is not what is from the properties of bodies. Rather, motion is a quality occurring in the consonant letter by inclining its point of articulation to the point of articulation of one of the vowel letters: to the alif is fatha, to the waw is damma, and to the ya is kasra. There is no disagreement about the impossibility of beginning with a vowel. The disagreement is only over whether that is due to its being quiescent so that beginning with a quiescent consonant is also impossible, or due to its essence because it is a prolongation generated from saturating a homologous motion, so it is inconceivable except where there is before it a vocalized consonant. This is the truth, because every sound-natured person finds from himself the possibility of beginning with a quiescent, even if it is rejected in the Arabic language, like stopping on a vocalized and combining two quiescent consonants except in stopping like Zayd and 'Amr, or when the first consonant is a soft letter and the second is assimilated like Khuwayṣa, for it is permissible, as when the first is a vowel like dābbah. The inability of some to begin with a quiescent does not prove its impossibility, like the pronunciation of some letters, for that is due to a deficiency in the faculty. The argument for possibility is that the vowel wherever it is is conditioned by a consonant. If the consonant were conditioned by it in some situations like the beginning, a circularity would follow. But it is nothing, because the vowel is conditioned by that a consonant precedes it, and the consonant in the beginning is conditioned by that a vowel follows it, so they are together, and there is no impossibility in that.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage defines the three vowel letters (alif, waw, ya) as prolongations of the short vowel gestures (fatha, damma, kasra). It then distinguishes between vowels (musawwata) and consonants (samita), noting that waw and ya can be consonants when not preceded by a homologous vowel. The author clarifies that 'motion' (haraka) and 'quiescence' (sukun) here are not physical properties but phonetic qualities: motion is the inclination of a consonant's articulation towards one of the three vowel articulations. The main argument concerns the impossibility of beginning an utterance with a vowel. The author states that there is consensus on this impossibility, but disagreement on its cause. The correct view, according to the author, is that the impossibility is due to the vowel's essence as a prolongation requiring a preceding vocalized consonant, not because it is quiescent. He supports this by noting that beginning with a quiescent consonant is inherently possible—as felt intuitively—even if it is not practiced in Arabic except in specific cases like pausing or assimilation. Inability to pronounce such beginnings is due to individual speech defects. He refutes a circularity objection: the vowel requires a preceding consonant, and the consonant at the beginning requires a following vowel, but this is mutual simultaneous dependency without circularity.

Key Terms
مصوتة (musawwata)
صامتة (samita)
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'motion' (haraka) mean in the context of vowels and consonants?
A) Physical movement of the tongue
B) A quality in the consonant that inclines its articulation towards a vowel
C) The presence of a vowel after a consonant
D) The lengthening of a vowel

Key Takeaway
The prolonged vowels are alif, waw, and ya when they are prolongations of fatha, damma, and kasra; motion is the phonetic inclination of a consonant towards a vowel.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you consider that each letter's 'motion' is a precise inclination toward a specific vowel? This lesson calls you to go beyond simply pronouncing sounds and to understand the exact articulatory gestures. Will you commit to studying the makharij so that your recitation is not just correct but deeply conscious?
?
The passage insists that الحركة (motion) is not a physical movement but a phonetic quality. Are you content with superficial understanding, or will you dig into the precise definitions of the letters you utter daily? This precision is an amanah (trust) on your tongue. How will you honor that trust in your next recitation?

The Impossibility of Beginning with a Vowel
Discusses the impossibility of beginning with a vowel, its cause, and refutes circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus that one cannot begin an utterance with a vowel, the disagreement over its cause, and the author's argument that it is due to the vowel's nature as a prolongation requiring a preceding consonant.

Core Idea
A vowel cannot begin an utterance because it is a prolongation dependent on a preceding consonant.

Having defined the phonetic terms, the author now addresses a key rule: it is impossible to start an utterance with a vowel. He states that there is consensus on this point, but scholars differ on the reason. He presents the correct view and supports it with intuitive and logical arguments, also refuting a potential circularity objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus and the two views
Read the passage to find the statement of consensus and the two proposed reasons for the impossibility of beginning with a vowel.
The disagreement is between whether it is due to quiescence or due to the vowel's essence.
2
Understand the correct view
The author argues that the impossibility is because a vowel is a prolongation (madda) that requires a preceding vocalized consonant. He supports this by noting that beginning with a quiescent consonant is intuitively possible.
He gives examples where beginning with a quiescent consonant is allowed in Arabic.
3
Examine the refutation of circularity
The author answers an objection that if beginning with a vowel requires a preceding consonant, and that consonant also requires a following vowel, that would be circular. He says it is mutual simultaneity, not circularity.
Both conditions can be satisfied simultaneously without paradox.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعني الألف من الفتحة والواو من الضمة، والياء من الكسرة تسمى المصوتة الممدودة، وهي وما سوى المصوتة تسمى صامتة، ويندرج فيها الواو والياء المتحركتان أو الساكنتان، إذ لم يكن قبل الواو ضمة وقبل الياء كسرة وأما الألف فلا يكون إلا مصوتاً، وإطلاقها على الهمزة باشتراك الاسم، وليس المراد بالحركة والسكون هاهنا ما هو من خواص الأجسام، بل الحركة عبارة عن كيفية حاصلة في الحرف الصامت من إمالة مخرجه إلى مخرج إحدى المدات، فإلى الألف فتحة وإلى الواو ضمة. وإلى الياء كسرة . ولا خلاف في امتناع الابتداء بالمصوت وإنما الخلاف في أن ذلك بسكونه حتى يمتنع الابتداء بالساكن الصامت أيضاً أو لذاته لكونه عبارة عن مدة متولدة من إشباع حركة تجانسها فلا يتصور إلا حيث يكون قبلها صامت متحرك وهذا هو الحق لأن كل سليم الحس يجد من نفسه إمكان الابتداء بالساكن، وإن كان مرفوضاً في لغة العرب كالوقف على المتحرك والجمع بين الساكنين من الصوامت إلا في الوقف مثل زيد وعمرو، أو إذا كان الصامت الأول حرف لين والثاني مدغماً نحو خويصة فإنه جائز، كما إذا كان الأول مصوتاً نحو دابة وعدم قدرة البعض على الابتداء بالساكن لا يدل على امتناعه كالتلفظ ببعض الحروف فإن ذلك لقصور في الآلة والاستدلال على الإمكان بأن المصوت أينما كان مشروط بالصامت، فلو كان الصامت مشروطاً به في بعض المواضع كالابتداء لزم الدور، وليس بشيء لأن المصوت مشروط بأن يسبقه صامت والصامت في الابتداء مشروط بأن يلحقه مصوت فيكونان معاً ولا إستحالة فيه.
I mean: the alif from the fatha, the waw from the damma, and the ya from the kasra are called the prolonged vowels (al-musawwata al-mamduda). And these and what is other than the vowel are called consonants (samita). Included among them are the waw and ya that are either vocalized or quiescent, when there is not before the waw a damma nor before the ya a kasra. As for the alif, it is only a vowel. Their application to the hamza is by homonymy. What is meant here by motion (haraka) and quiescence (sukun) is not what is from the properties of bodies. Rather, motion is a quality occurring in the consonant letter by inclining its point of articulation to the point of articulation of one of the vowel letters: to the alif is fatha, to the waw is damma, and to the ya is kasra. There is no disagreement about the impossibility of beginning with a vowel. The disagreement is only over whether that is due to its being quiescent so that beginning with a quiescent consonant is also impossible, or due to its essence because it is a prolongation generated from saturating a homologous motion, so it is inconceivable except where there is before it a vocalized consonant. This is the truth, because every sound-natured person finds from himself the possibility of beginning with a quiescent, even if it is rejected in the Arabic language, like stopping on a vocalized and combining two quiescent consonants except in stopping like Zayd and 'Amr, or when the first consonant is a soft letter and the second is assimilated like Khuwayṣa, for it is permissible, as when the first is a vowel like dābbah. The inability of some to begin with a quiescent does not prove its impossibility, like the pronunciation of some letters, for that is due to a deficiency in the faculty. The argument for possibility is that the vowel wherever it is is conditioned by a consonant. If the consonant were conditioned by it in some situations like the beginning, a circularity would follow. But it is nothing, because the vowel is conditioned by that a consonant precedes it, and the consonant in the beginning is conditioned by that a vowel follows it, so they are together, and there is no impossibility in that.

Commentary
This passage defines the three vowel letters (alif, waw, ya) as prolongations of the short vowel gestures (fatha, damma, kasra). It then distinguishes between vowels (musawwata) and consonants (samita), noting that waw and ya can be consonants when not preceded by a homologous vowel. The author clarifies that 'motion' (haraka) and 'quiescence' (sukun) here are not physical properties but phonetic qualities: motion is the inclination of a consonant's articulation towards one of the three vowel articulations. The main argument concerns the impossibility of beginning an utterance with a vowel. The author states that there is consensus on this impossibility, but disagreement on its cause. The correct view, according to the author, is that the impossibility is due to the vowel's essence as a prolongation requiring a preceding vocalized consonant, not because it is quiescent. He supports this by noting that beginning with a quiescent consonant is inherently possible—as felt intuitively—even if it is not practiced in Arabic except in specific cases like pausing or assimilation. Inability to pronounce such beginnings is due to individual speech defects. He refutes a circularity objection: the vowel requires a preceding consonant, and the consonant at the beginning requires a following vowel, but this is mutual simultaneous dependency without circularity.

Key Terms
ابتداء (ibtida')
مصوت (musawwat)
مدة (madda)
صامت (samit)
دور (dawr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the impossibility of beginning with a vowel is because it is quiescent (sakin).
Correction: The author argues it is because the vowel is a prolongation (madda) that requires a preceding vocalized consonant, not because it is quiescent.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible to begin an utterance with a vowel?
A) Because a vowel is always quiescent.
B) Because a vowel is a prolongation that requires a preceding vocalized consonant.
C) Because the Arabic language forbids it.
D) Because no person can physically produce a vowel at the start.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of beginning with a vowel is due to its nature as a prolongation requiring a preceding consonant, not because it is quiescent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author writes that a vowel cannot initiate speech because it is a prolongation that depends on something preceding it. Consider your own speech: do you ever speak without first being prompted by a thought or intention? How does this phonetic truth mirror the principle that every effect has a cause? What does this imply about the necessity of a First Cause for your existence?
?
The passage refutes the circularity objection by showing that mutual dependence can be simultaneous rather than vicious. Are there beliefs you hold that seem circular? Have you ever dismissed a truth because it appeared to require circular reasoning without investigating further? What would it take for you to examine such a belief with the same logical rigor shown here?

Division of Letters into Temporal and Instantaneous
Divides letters into temporal and instantaneous based on prolongation possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between temporal and instantaneous letters in Arabic phonetics, including the special case where instantaneous letters appear prolonged due to rapid repetition.

Core Idea
Letters are either temporal (prolongable) or instantaneous (not), but some sounds seem longer due to rapid repetition.

In Arabic phonetics, letters are classified not only by their points of articulation but also by their temporal extension. Some letters can be held continuously, while others cannot. This classification also reveals a fascinating phenomenon: certain instantaneous letters, like ḥā' and khā', sound prolonged to the ear but are actually composed of many rapid repetitions. Let's explore the passage to understand these categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's classification come through before you interpret.
The passage uses technical terms like ānī and zamānī.
2
Identify the two main categories
Find where the author divides letters into temporal and instantaneous. Note the defining criterion: possibility of prolongation.
Example: fā' is temporal because it can be prolonged; ṭā' is instantaneous because it cannot.
3
Examine the subtypes of instantaneous
The passage explains that instantaneous letters occur either at the beginning of breath release or at the end of breath retention. Look at the examples ṭala'a and ghalīṭ.
Also note that middle letters (like in baṭala) can be ambiguous.
4
Understand the special case
The author describes letters like ḥā' and khā' that seem temporal but are actually instantaneous. Understand how rapid repetition creates the illusion of prolongation.
This is a key nuance in the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وينقسم أي الحرف باعتبار آخر إلى آني وزماني لأنه إن أمكن تمديده كالفاء فزماني وإن لم يمكن كالطاء فآني، وهو إنما يوجد في أول زمان إرسال النفس كما في طلع، أو في آخر زمان حبسه كما في غلط. وما يقع في وسط الكلمة مثل بطل يحتمل الأمرين، وعروض الآني للصوت يكون بمعنى إنه طرف له كالنقطة للخط، ومن الآني ما يشبه الزماني كالحاء والخاء ونحوهما مما لا يمكن تحديده، لكن تجتمع عند التلفظ بواحد منها أفراد متماثلة، ولا يشعر الحس بامتياز زمان بعضها عن بعض فيظن حرفاً واحداً.
His statement: 'And it divides' – i.e., the letter from another consideration – into instantaneous (ānī) and temporal (zamānī). Because if its prolongation is possible, like the fā', it is temporal; and if it is not possible, like the ṭā', it is instantaneous. And it [the instantaneous] occurs only at the first time of the release of breath, as in ṭala'a, or at the last time of its retention, as in ghalīṭ. And what occurs in the middle of a word, like baṭala, admits both possibilities. The occurrence of the instantaneous for sound is in the sense that it is an endpoint for it, like the point for the line. Among the instantaneous is what resembles the temporal, like the ḥā' and khā' and similar letters that cannot be delimited, but when pronouncing one of them, similar individuals come together, and the sense does not perceive the distinction of the time of some from others, so one supposes it to be a single letter.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of letters from a different viewpoint: instantaneous versus temporal. A letter is temporal (zamānī) if it can be prolonged, like the fā'. It is instantaneous (ānī) if it cannot be prolonged, like the ṭā'. The instantaneous letter occurs either at the very beginning of the release of breath (e.g., ṭā' in ṭala'a) or at the very end of its retention (e.g., ṭā' in ghalīṭ). Letters in the middle of a word, like the ṭā' in baṭala, could be interpreted either way. The author compares the instantaneous letter to a point on a line—it is an endpoint of the sound. He further notes that some instantaneous letters resemble temporal ones, like ḥā' and khā'. These letters cannot actually be delimited, but when pronounced, multiple similar individuals occur in rapid succession, and the ear perceives them as a single prolonged letter due to the imperceptible distinctions between their times.

Key Terms
آني (ānī)
زماني (zamānī)
تمديد (tamdīd)
إرسال النفس (irsāl al-nafs)
حبس النفس (ḥabs al-nafs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that letters like ḥā' and khā' are temporal because they can be prolonged in pronunciation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that these letters are actually instantaneous; the apparent prolongation is an illusion caused by rapid repetition of identical instantaneous sounds.

Checkpoint
Which letter is given as an example of a purely instantaneous letter?
A) Fā'
B) Ṭā'
C) Ḥā'
D) Bā'

Key Takeaway
A letter is temporal if it can be prolonged; instantaneous if not, and some instantaneous letters create an illusion of prolongation through rapid repetition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that some instantaneous letters appear prolonged due to rapid repetition, deceiving the ear. In your spiritual life, are there actions or habits that appear to be continuous acts of devotion but are actually just repeated momentary deeds without depth? How will you examine the individual 'instants' of your prayers and dhikr to ensure they are genuine, not mere repetitions?
?
The instantaneous letter exists only at the boundary of breath release or retention—a single moment that marks an endpoint. Consider your life: what 'endpoints' are you currently approaching? Are you living each moment as if it could be the last instant before your breath is retained forever? How does this classification of letters remind you of the precarious nature of your own existence?
?
The author distinguishes between temporal letters, which can be stretched, and instantaneous ones, which cannot. Reflect on your own character: are there qualities you treat as 'temporal'—thinking you can prolong them indefinitely—when in reality they are 'instantaneous' opportunities that must be seized now? What specific good deed are you postponing as if it were prolongable, when it may only be possible at this immediate instant?

Division of Letters into Similar and Different
Classifies letters into similar/different by essence or accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how letters are classified as similar or different based on essential vs. accidental features, and the two kinds of accidental differences.

Core Idea
Letters differ either in their very nature or in accidental qualities.

This passage extends the analysis of letters by introducing a new classification: similar and different. The author distinguishes between differences that are essential (different letter types) and those that are accidental (e.g., vocalization or position). Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing the nature of linguistic units.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Identify the two main categories: similar letters and different letters.
2
Distinguish types of difference
Note that difference can be by essence (different letters like bā' and tā') or by accident (same letter with different accidental qualities).
3
Understand accidental features
Recognize the two kinds of accidents: specifying (related to speaker or time) and non-specifying (like vowel quality).
The passage ends mid-sentence but the classification is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم آخر للحروف إلى متماثل ومختلفة قال: وإلى متماثل كالبائين الساكنين، ومختلف بالذات كالباء والتاء، أو بالعرض كبائين متحرك وساكن بعده ، أو مضموم مفتوح . يريد أن الحروف التسعة والعشرين الواقعة في لغة العرب وما سواها مما يقع في بعض اللغات أنواع مختلفة بالماهية، وقد يختلف أفراد كل منها بعوارض مشخصة كالياء الساكنة التي يتلفظ بها زيد الآن أو في وقت آخر أو يتلفظ بها عمرو، أو غير مشخصة كالياء الساكنة أو المتحركة بالفتحة أو الضمة أو الكسرة، فمع قطع النظر عن اللافظ تكون أفراد النوع الواحد، إما متحدة في السكون والحركة كاليائين الساكنتين أو المتحركتين بالفتحة أو الكسرة.
Another division of letters into similar and different. He said: 'And into similar, like two quiescent bā's; and different by essence, like bā' and tā'; or by accident, like two bā's one vocalized and the other quiescent after it, or one with damma and one with fatha.' He intends that the twenty-nine letters occurring in the Arabic language and others that occur in some languages are different types in essence. And the individuals of each type may differ by specifying accidents, like the quiescent yā' that Zayd pronounces now or at another time or that 'Amr pronounces, or by non-specifying accidents, like the quiescent yā' or the yā' vocalized with fatha, damma, or kasra. So, disregarding the speaker, the individuals of a single type are either united in quiescence and motion, like two quiescent yā's or two vocalized with fatha or kasra.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents another classification of letters: similar and different. Similar letters are those that are identical in their essential qualities, e.g., two quiescent bā's. Different letters can be different by essence (e.g., bā' and tā' are different letters) or different by accident (e.g., two bā's where one is vocalized and the other quiescent, or one with damma and one with fatha). The author then explains that the twenty-nine Arabic letters (and additional letters from other languages) are essentially different types. Within each type, individual occurrences may vary due to accidental features. These accidents can be specifying (e.g., uttered by Zayd vs. 'Amr, or at different times) or non-specifying (e.g., whether the letter is quiescent or vocalized with a particular vowel). When we disregard the speaker, individuals of the same type can be either identical in terms of being quiescent or having the same vowel (e.g., two quiescent yā's) or different in those accidental features.

Key Terms
متماثل (mutamāthil)
مختلف (mukhtalif)
بالذات (bi-dh-dhāt)
بالعرض (bi-l-'araḍ)
عوارض مشخصة (ʿawāriḍ mushakhkhiṣa)
عوارض غير مشخصة (ʿawāriḍ ghayr mushakhkhiṣa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that different accidental features make the letter itself different in essence.
Correction: The author distinguishes essential difference (different letter types) from accidental difference (same letter type with varying non-essential qualities).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes two letters different by accident?
A) They are different letters like bā' and tā'.
B) They differ in a non-essential quality like being vocalized vs. quiescent.
C) They are spoken by different people.
D) They belong to different languages.

Key Takeaway
Letters can be classified as similar or different, with essential differences defining letter types and accidental differences representing variations within a type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you interact with others, do you tend to focus on accidental differences like their tone, appearance, or background (like the different vowels of letters), rather than their essential humanity? Choose one person today and commit to seeing them first as a creature of Allah, beyond accidents.
?
The author distinguishes specifying accidents (like who speaks) from non-specifying ones (like vowels). In your relationships, do you ever let who said something (specifying) or how it was said (non-specifying) overshadow the essential message? Name one situation where you will consciously separate the message from the medium.
?
Reflect on a belief or practice in your faith: are you prioritizing an accidental detail (e.g., a particular method) over the core essence (e.g., sincerity or intention)? Write down one area where you will realign your focus from accident to essence starting today.

Definition of Speech and Its Basic Divisions
Author defines speech and divides it into simple and compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of speech (الكلام) and its primary division into simple and compound, along with the subdivisions of each.

Core Idea
Speech is defined as meaningful compositions of distinct audible letters.

This lesson establishes the foundational definitions of speech and its categories. Before we can discuss theological arguments, we must agree on what constitutes meaningful speech. The author begins by defining الكلام as a composition of letters that convey meaning, then divides it into its basic types.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the passage focusing on how the author defines الكلام: a composition of audible, distinct letters. Note the conditions 'audible' and 'distinct' that exclude written or imagined forms and bird sounds.
2
Identify the divisions
The passage divides speech first into meaningless and meaningful, then meaningful into simple and compound. Trace each division and note the further subdivisions of simple (noun, verb, particle) and compound (complete, incomplete).
Pay attention to the terms: مفرد, مركب, تام, غير تام.
3
Apply the definitions
Try to classify a few Arabic words using the divisions: e.g., 'زيد' (noun, simple), 'قائم' (adjective? but it's a noun in Arabic? Actually it's an active participle, treated as a noun), 'في' (particle, simple), 'زيد قائم' (compound, complete). This reinforces the taxonomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالاعتبار الأول مجرد صامت و مصوت ممدود ليس بينهما مصوت مقصور على ما يراه أهل العربية من أن لا لام وألف بينهما فتحة، وذلك لأن المصوت الممدود ليس إلا إشباعاً للمصوت المقصور فيكون المقصور مندرجاً في الممدود جزءاً منه وهذا ما يقال إن الحركات أبعاض حروف المد فلا يكون إلا صامتاً مع مصوت ممدود الحروف ومكونات اللفظ والكلام والمفرد والمركب (قال: ويتألف من الحروف الكلام المنقسم إلى المفرد والمركب بأقسامهما، ويسمى اللفظ أيضاً، وقد يخص الكلام بما يفيد ولو كان مقطعاً مثل ، وقى(۱) ، واللفظ بما يتألف من المقاطع، وبهذا يقع في مقابلة الحرف والمقطع حيث يقال أجزاء المركب ألفاظ أو حروف أو مقاطع). ق، ويفسر بالمنتظم من الحروف المسموعة المتميزة، ويحترز بالمسموعة عن المكتوبة أو المتخيلة وبالمتميزة عن أصوات الطيور، والكلام ينقسم إلى المهمل ،والموضوع والموضوع إلى المفرد والمركب، والمفرد (۲) إلى الاسم والفعل والحرف والمركب إلى التام الذي يصح السكوت عليه، وإلى غير التام، واللفظ أعم من الحروف (۳) والكلام . وقد يخص الكلام باللفظ المفيد بمعنى دلالته على نسبة يصح السكوت عليها سواء كانت إنشائية مثل : قم وهل زيد قائم؟، ولعل زيداً قائم؟؟ ونحو ذلك أو إخبارية مثل: زيد قائم، وسواء كان اللفظ مقطعاً مقصوراً مثل ق أو ممدوداً مثل فى وقو أو مركباً من المقاطع كما ذكر وقد يخص اللفظ بما يتألف من المقاطع فيقابله () الحرف والمقطع ولذا يقال : أجزاء المركب ألفاظ أو حروف أو مقاطع فزيد قائم من لفظين وماذا من مقطعين ويا زيد من مقطع، ولفظ درى(ه) في أمر المخاطبة من مقطع وحرف وارضى وأخشوا من لفظ وحرف ويشكل بمثل في وقو فإن كلا منهما مقطع ممدود فقط إلا أن يقال : إنه من حرفين صامت ومصوت، وأما مثل ق فمن مقطع مقصور، ولفظ هو الضمير المستتر أعني أنت وهذا بخلاف قي وقو فإن كلاً من الياء والواو اسم (۱) ولا مستتر هناك .
And by the first consideration, a pure consonant and a long vowel with no short vowel between them, according to the view of the Arabic grammarians that there is no lām and alif with a fatḥa between them, because the long vowel is nothing but an extension of the short vowel, so the short vowel is included in the long vowel as part of it. This is what is said: the vowels are parts of the letters of prolongation, so there is only a consonant with a long vowel. Letters and components of utterance, speech, simple and compound. (He said: Speech is composed of letters, divided into simple and compound with their divisions, and it is also called utterance. Kalam may be specified as that which conveys a meaning even if it is a syllable, such as 'qā(1)', and utterance as that which is composed of syllables, and by this it is contrasted with the letter and syllable, as it is said: the parts of a compound are utterances or letters or syllables.) q, and it is interpreted as the regular sequence of distinct audible letters, guarding by 'audible' against written or imagined, and by 'distinct' against bird sounds. Speech is divided into meaningless and meaningful, and meaningful into simple and compound. Simple is divided into noun, verb, and particle. Compound is divided into complete, upon which it is permissible to stop, and incomplete. Utterance is more general than letters and speech. And kalam may be specified as useful utterance in the sense of indicating a relation upon which it is permissible to stop, whether it is performative such as 'Stand!' and 'Is Zayd standing?' and 'Perhaps Zayd is standing?' and the like, or declarative such as 'Zayd is standing.' And whether the utterance is a short syllable like 'q' or a long one like 'fī' and 'qū' or compounded of syllables as mentioned. And utterance may be specified as that which is composed of syllables, contrasted with the letter and syllable. Hence it is said: the parts of a compound are utterances or letters or syllables. Thus 'Zayd is standing' consists of two utterances, 'mā dhā' of two syllables, 'yā Zayd' of one syllable, and the word 'darā(5)' in the command of address consists of a syllable and a letter, and 'irḍā' and 'akhshaw' consist of an utterance and a letter. This is problematic with examples like 'fī' and 'qū', for each of them is only a long syllable, unless it is said that it is composed of two letters: a consonant and a vowel. As for 'q', it is a short syllable. And the word 'huwa' is the implicit pronoun, meaning 'anta', and this is different from 'qī' and 'qū', for each of the yā and wāw is a noun, and there is no implicit pronoun there.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines key linguistic terms used in logic and kalam. It begins by discussing the structure of syllables (مقاطع), distinguishing between a consonant with a long vowel and the absence of a short vowel, as per Arabic grammar. The long vowel is an extension of the short vowel, so the short vowel is implicit. Then it moves to definitions: الحروف (letters) are basic units; اللفظ (utterance) is broader; الكلام (speech) is a meaningful composition of letters, divided into simple (مفرد) and compound (مركب). Simple is further divided into noun, verb, particle; compound into complete (تام) and incomplete. الكلام can be specified as useful speech indicating a relation, whether performative or declarative. The author also explains syllables as measuring units, giving examples like 'q' (short syllable) and 'fī' (long syllable), and the implicit pronoun. This lays the groundwork for later arguments about the quantitative nature of utterance.

Key Terms
كلام
لفظ
مفرد
مركب
تام
غير تام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that any sequence of letters qualifies as speech (كلام) in the technical sense.
Correction: The passage specifies that speech must be composed of distinct, audible letters and must be meaningful (or at least capable of being meaningful in the division). Meaningless sequences are considered speech only in a broader sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is meaningful speech (الموضوع) divided?
A) Into simple and compound
B) Into noun and verb
C) Into complete and incomplete
D) Into audible and written

Key Takeaway
Speech (الكلام) is defined as a meaningful composition of distinct audible letters, and it is systematically divided into simple (noun, verb, particle) and compound (complete/incomplete) categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Distinction Between Utterance and Speech
Author distinguishes utterance from speech and explains syllable measurement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between utterance (لفظ) and speech (كلام), and how utterances are measured by syllables.

Core Idea
Utterance is more general than speech; speech is a subset of utterance that conveys a complete meaning.

Having learned the basic divisions of speech, we now turn to the broader concept of utterance. The author clarifies that utterance includes both meaningful and meaningless sequences, and introduces the syllable as the unit of measurement for utterances. This distinction is crucial for analyzing the composition of language in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on utterance
Read the passage starting from 'واللفظ أعم من الحروف والكلام'. Notice that utterance (لفظ) is said to be more general than both letters and speech. Understand that speech is a special kind of utterance.
2
Understand the two specifications of speech
The author presents two ways to specify speech: as a useful utterance that indicates a relation (whether performative or declarative), and as composed of syllables. Identify the examples given for each.
3
Analyze the syllable examples
The passage gives examples of syllables: short (مقصور) like 'q', long (ممدود) like 'fī' and 'qū', and compounded. Work through the examples of 'Zayd qā'im', 'mā dhā', 'yā Zayd', etc., to see how syllables and letters combine.
Pay attention to the distinction between a long syllable and a letter.

Source Text

Defining Life: A Power Requiring Sensation and Movement
The author defines life in the sensed world and discusses different interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of life in the witnessed world according to classical kalam, and how it differs from related powers like sensation, movement, and nutrition.

Core Idea
Life is the principle behind sensation and movement, not the powers themselves.

In classical Islamic theology, defining the attributes of living beings is essential before discussing God's attributes. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid focuses on the definition of life (al-ḥayāh) as it applies to created beings. The author clarifies that life is not identical to the power of sensation and movement but is their principle, and then presents various ways scholars have expressed this reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the distinction between life and the power of sensation/movement.
The text has OCR issues, but the core meaning is clear.
2
Identify the three definitions
The passage presents three definitions of life. Find each one and note how they differ in wording but all refer to the same reality.
Look for phrases starting with 'فَقِيلَ'.
3
Understand the distinction from nutrition
The author explains that life is distinct from the power of nutrition using the examples of plants and paralyzed limbs. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
Nutrition exists in plants without life, so they are separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحياة مبدأ لقوة الحس والحركة
(قال : فمنها الحياة(۱) وهي (۲) في الشاهد قوة تقتضي الحس والحركة أي تكون مبدأ لقوة الحس والحركة، وهذا معنى قولهم قوة تتبع اعتدال النوع وتفيض عنها سائر القوى أي القوى الحيوانية فتكون غير قوة الحس والحركة لتغاير المبدأ وذي (۳) المبدأ، وغير قوة التغذية لوجودها في النبات مع عدم الحياة، ولهذا كان في العضو المفلوج، أو الذابل الحياة بأثرها من غير حس وحركة أو اغتداء) . عسر صفة سيجيء معنى الحياة في حق الله تعالى وأما حياة الحي من الأجسام فقد اختلفت العبارات في تفسيرها لا من جهة اختلاف في حقيقتها، بل من جهة الاطلاع عليها والتعبير عنها إلا باعتبار اللوازم والآثار، فقيل : هي تقتضي الحس والحركة مشروطة باعتدال المزاج ، والقيد الأخير للتحقيق على ما رأي البعض لا للاحتراز وقيل قوة (٤) هي مبدأ لقوة الحس والحركة، وكان هذا هو المراد بالأول ليتميز عن قوة الحس والحركة وقيل قوة تتبع اعتدال النوع ، هو
Life is a principle for the power of sensation and movement. (He said: Among them is life, and it in the witnessed world is a power that requires sensation and movement, i.e., it is a principle for the power of sensation and movement. And this is the meaning of their statement: a power that follows the balance of the species and from which the other powers flow, i.e., the animal powers, so it is other than the power of sensation and movement because of the difference between the principle and that which has the principle, and other than the power of nutrition because it exists in plants without life, and for this reason in a paralyzed or withered limb, life exists with its effect without sensation, movement, or nutrition.) [عسر صفة] The meaning of life with respect to God will come later. As for the life of living beings among bodies, the expressions differ in its interpretation not due to a difference in its reality, but due to the way of perceiving and expressing it, except by considering the necessary implications and effects. So it is said: It requires sensation and movement conditioned by balanced temperament, and the last restriction is for verification according to some, not for exclusion. And it is said: It is a power that is a principle for the power of sensation and movement, and this was intended by the first to distinguish it from the power of sensation and movement. And it is said: A power that follows the balance of the species, [هو]
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines the quality of life (al-ḥayāh) as one of the psychical qualities. The author begins by stating that life in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) is a power that requires sensation and movement, being a principle from which the power of sensation and movement originates. He clarifies that life is not identical to the power of sensation and movement, because the principle (life) is distinct from that which has the principle (the power of sensation). Similarly, life is distinct from the power of nutrition, since nutrition exists in plants which lack life. This is illustrated by a paralyzed or withered limb, where life is present with its effect but without sensation or movement. The author then notes that the meaning of life with respect to God will be discussed later. For living bodies, various expressions have been used to define life, differing not in the reality of life but in how it is perceived and expressed based on its necessary implications and effects. Three definitions are presented: (1) Life is that which requires sensation and movement conditioned by balanced temperament (with the condition being for verification rather than exclusion). (2) Life is a power that is a principle for the power of sensation and movement (this is said to be the intended meaning of the first definition, to distinguish life from the power of sensation and movement). (3) Life is a power that follows the balance of the species. The passage ends abruptly, possibly continuing on the next page.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life is the same as the power of sensation and movement.
Correction: Life is the principle that gives rise to the power of sensation and movement, not the power itself.

Checkpoint

B) Because nutrition requires sensation and movement.
C) Because life is a principle for both sensation and nutrition.
D) Because nutrition is a type of psychical quality.

Key Takeaway
Life in the witnessed world is a principle for sensation and movement, distinct from those powers and from nutrition, and can be expressed in various ways that all point to the same reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that life is a principle distinct from your ability to sense and move. When you lie down to sleep tonight, you will lose sensation and movement temporarily. Do you truly believe that your life—your soul's principle—continues, entrusted to the One who gives it? If so, how should that trust change the anxiety you carry into your pillow?
?
You have likely treated your physical abilities—seeing, hearing, walking—as if they were your life itself. But the lesson says life is the source, not the effect. When you lose one of those abilities through injury or age, will you still know that your life, your very self, remains intact and valuable? How can you prepare your heart now for that inevitable test?
?

Do you see them as fully alive, or do you unconsciously diminish their personhood because of their limitations? What specific change in your treatment of them will you make today to honor the principle of life within them?

Two Definitions of Death: Privation vs. Positive Quality
Presents two definitions of death: privation vs. positive quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two principal definitions of death in Islamic theology—privation of life and a positive quality opposing life—and the key distinctions between them.

Core Idea
Death is either the privation of life from a once-living being or a created quality opposing life.

Before we can discuss deeper theological questions about life and death, we need a clear definition of what 'death' means. In this passage, the author lays out two competing definitions found in Islamic thought: one viewing death as the absence of life in a subject that previously possessed it, and another treating it as a positive created quality. Understanding these definitions is essential for grasping later discussions on divine action and the nature of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once without stopping. Let the author's own phrasing and flow settle in your mind before analyzing.
2
Identify the two main definitions
The passage presents two core definitions of death: one as the departure/non-existence of life, the other as a quality opposing life. Find where each is introduced.
3
Understand the privation view deeply
Focus on the first definition and its explanation. Note the concept of 'adam malaka (privation of a positive state) and how it differs from absolute non-existence.
The example of blindness after sight versus congenital blindness is key.
4
Examine the positive quality view
Now look at the second definition: death as a positive quality opposing life. See how this connects to the Mu'tazilite claim that death is an act of God or an angel.
5
Synthesize the two views
Compare and contrast the two definitions. What does each imply about God's role in causing death? Why is the distinction between death and killing important?
The author's careful use of 'effect' versus 'effecting' matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما الموت فزوال الحياة أي عدمها عما اتصف بها، أو كيفية تضادها، وكأن هذا مراد من قال : إنه فعل من الله تعالى أو من الملك يقتضي زوال حياة الجسم من غير جرح احتراز عن القتل على أن الفعل بمعنى الأثر إذ التأثير إماتة، فعلى الأول معنى خلقه تقديره، أو خلق أسبابه . فزوال الحياة ومعنى زوال الصفة عدمها عما يتصف بها بالفعل وهذا معنى ما قيل إنه عدم الحياة عما من شأنه أي عما يكون من أمره وصفته الحياة بالفعل فيكون عدم ملكة للحياة كما في العمى الطاريء بعد البصر لا كمطلق العمى، ولا يلزم كون عدم الحياة عن الجنين عند استعداده للحياة موتاً فعلى هذا لا يكون الموت عدمياً، وقيل : هو كيفية تضاد الحياة فيكون وجودياً، وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل ما ذكره المعتزلة من أن الموت فعل من الله تعالى، أو من الملك يقتضي زوال حياة الجسم من غير جرح . واحترز بالقيد الأخير عن القتل وحمل الفعل على الكيفية المضادة مبني على أن المراد به الأثر الصادر عن الفاعل إذ لو أريد به التأثير على ما هو الظاهر لكان ذلك تفسيراً للإماتة لا للموت.
He said: As for death, it is the departure of life, i.e., its non-existence from that which was qualified by it, or a quality opposing it. And this seems to be the intended meaning of those who said: it is an act of God Most High or of the angel that necessitates the departure of life from the body without a wound, as a precaution against killing, on the basis that 'act' here means effect, since the effecting is causing death. So on the first [interpretation], the meaning of His 'creating' it is His preordainment of it, or His creation of its causes. So the departure of life and the meaning of the departure of the attribute is its non-existence from that which is actually qualified by it, and this is the meaning of what was said: it is the non-existence of life from that which is such, i.e., from that whose affair and attribute is life actually, so it is a privation of a positive state of life, like blindness that occurs after sight, not like absolute blindness. And it does not follow that the non-existence of life from the embryo at the time of its readiness for life is death, so on this view death is not a privation. And it was said: it is a quality opposing life, so it is positive. And on this should be interpreted what the Mu'tazila mentioned, that death is an act of God Most High or of the angel that necessitates the departure of life from the body without a wound. And he guarded by the last restriction against killing, and the interpretation of 'act' as the opposing quality is based on the view that what is meant by it is the effect emanating from the agent; for if what was meant by it were the effecting, which is the apparent meaning, that would be an interpretation of 'causing death' not of 'death'.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'death' (al-mawt) by presenting two main views found in theological discussions. The author begins by stating that death is either the departure of life, meaning the non-existence of life from that which was qualified by it, or a quality that opposes life. He notes that this second view corresponds to those who say death is an act of God or an angel that removes life from the body without a wound, distinguishing it from killing. The author clarifies that 'act' here means the effect, because the 'effecting' is actually causing death. On the first view, God's 'creation' of death means His preordainment (taqdīr) of it or His creation of its causes. The author then elaborates on the privation view, explaining that the departure of life is the non-existence of the attribute of life from that which actually possesses it, making it a privation of a positive state (ʿadam malaka), like blindness after sight rather than congenital blindness. He emphasizes that on this view, the absence of life in an embryo not yet ready for life is not considered death, so death is not a mere privation but a privation of a positive state. The second view holds that death is a positive quality opposing life, and on this basis one should interpret the Mu'tazilite statement that death is an act of God or an angel. The author adds a caution: the phrase 'without a wound' guards against killing, and interpreting 'act' as the quality is based on it being the effect, not the action, because otherwise it would define 'causing death' rather than 'death'.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is simply the non-existence of life without any qualification.
Correction: The passage clarifies that on the privation view, death is specifically the non-existence of life from a subject that previously possessed it (ʿadam malaka), not absolute non-existence. An embryo not yet having life does not 'die' in this sense.

Checkpoint

A) Death is the absolute non-existence of life in any subject.
B) Death is the non-existence of life in that which was previously characterized by life.
C) Death is an eternal non-existence.
D) Death is a positive quality created by God.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic theology, death is not simply the absence of life; it is defined either as the privation of life from a being that once possessed it or as a positive quality created by God that opposes life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage takes great care to define death precisely, showing that even scholars disagreed. Has your own understanding of death been vague or borrowed from others? Will you now commit to knowing exactly what it is, so that you can live with clarity and purpose?
?
Death is exclusive to beings who were once alive—it is not the state of never having lived. Have you been living as if you will never die, or as if death is a distant abstraction? What one concrete change will you make this week to remind yourself that your life is a gift that will be taken back?

Knowledge as Acquisition of Form in the Intellect
Defines knowledge as acquisition of form and relates it to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of knowledge as the acquisition of a form in the intellect and how it relates to the external essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a mental form that mirrors external reality without adding anything except mental consideration.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces the foundational definition of knowledge in Islamic theology. It clarifies that knowledge consists of an image (form) that the intellect acquires, and this acquisition is analogous to existence for essences. However, this mental form remains an accident in the mind without changing the substance of the known object.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of knowledge
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting how the author defines knowledge as the acquisition of a form. Pay attention to the distinction between perception and acquisition.
The author begins with a comment on perception before giving the core definition.
2
Understand the nature of scientific forms
The passage explains that scientific forms are accidents in the mind but do not negate the substantiality of the intelligible essence. Reflect on how a mental image can represent a substance without being a substance itself.
This is key to avoiding confusion between mental existence and external existence.
3
Analyze the relation of acquisition to form
The author compares the relation of acquisition (husul) to the form in the intellect to the relation of existence (wujud) to essence (mahiyya) externally. Note the claim that there is no increase except by mental consideration.
This means the mental form is not an added entity but a representation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صورته وإنكم تجعلون الإدراك للنفس، أو للحس المشترك مع أن الحصول في الجليدية مثلاً، وأنه ربما يتحقق الحصول فيها مع عدم الادراك لعدم التفات النفس، ثم الصور العلمية وإن كانت من حيث حصـولها في العقل عرضاً لم تناف كون الماهية المعقولة جوهراً بمعنى أنها إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع ، كما أنها تكون جزئياً لقيامها بالنفس الجزئية، وكلياً من حيث انشائها إلى الأفراد ونسبة الحصول إلى الصورة في العقل نسبة الوجود إلى الماهيه في الخارج فلا زيادة إلا باعتبار العقل، ومن قد يجعل العلم نفس الصورة فهي من حيث إن الحصول نفسها علم وعرض موجود في الأعيان كسائر صفات النفس، ومن حيث إنه زائد عليها مفهوم ولا تحقق له إلا في الأذهان، وأما المعلوم فهو مساله الصورة لا نفسها، إلا أن يستأنف لها تعقل ويلحقها أحكام هي المعقولات الثانية، وبهذا الاعتبار يصح جعل الكلية من عوارض المعلوم حقيقة .
Its form: and you make perception belong to the soul or to the common sense, while [it is possible that] the acquisition (ḥuṣūl) in the jelly [of the eye?] for example, and that perhaps the acquisition occurs in it without perception due to lack of attention of the soul. Then, the scientific forms (al-ṣuwar al-ʿilmiyya), although they are accidents (ʿaraḍ) by virtue of their occurrence in the intellect, do not negate that the intelligible essence (al-māhiyya al-maʿqūla) is a substance (jawhar) in the sense that if it existed externally it would not be in a subject. Also, it is particular (juzʾī) due to its subsistence in a particular soul, and universal (kullī) in terms of its relation to individuals. The relation of acquisition (ḥuṣūl) to the form in the intellect is like the relation of existence (wujūd) to the essence (māhiyya) externally, so there is no increase except by consideration of the intellect. Some make knowledge itself the form: from the viewpoint that the acquisition itself is knowledge, it is an accident existing in external beings like other attributes of the soul; from the viewpoint that it is additional to it, it is a concept that has no realization except in minds. As for the known object (al-maʿlūm), it is the bearer of the form, not the form itself, unless it is later subjected to intellection and attached to it rulings which are the second intelligibles (al-maʿqūlāt al-thāniya). By this consideration, it is valid to make universality (al-kulliyya) an accident of the known object in reality.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage defines knowledge (al-ʿilm) as the acquisition (ḥuṣūl) of a form (ṣūra) in the intellect. It begins by noting that perception is often attributed to the soul or the common sense, but acquisition can occur without perception due to inattention. The author then explains that scientific forms, though accidents in the mind, do not prevent the intelligible essence from being a substance if it existed externally. The form in the intellect is particular because it subsists in a particular soul, yet universal in relation to the individuals it represents. The relation of the form to the mind is like the relation of existence to essence: no addition except by mental consideration. Some philosophers consider knowledge to be the form itself: in one respect, the acquisition is knowledge and an accident like other attributes; in another, it is a concept additional to the form. The known object is the bearer of the form, not the form itself, unless the form is later reflected upon and becomes the object of second intelligibles. Thus universality is an accident of the known object in reality.

ماهية معقولة
عرض
جوهر
كلي
جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the mental form is identical to the external object or that it adds a new entity to the mind.
Correction: The form is an accident in the mind representing the essence; it does not substantiate it. The relation is like existence to essence, so no addition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation of acquisition (ḥuṣūl) to the form in the intellect?
A) It is like the relation of substance to accident.
B) It is like the relation of existence to essence externally.
C) It is the same as perception.
D) It is an accidental addition.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is the acquisition of a form in the intellect, which relates to the external essence as existence relates to essence, without any addition except mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a concept you believe you truly know, like the sun or your own existence. When you bring it to mind, do you feel that your mental image 'adds' something to the real thing? Based on this lesson, what is actually happening? How does this affect your humility about your knowledge?
?
The passage says acquisition can occur without perception due to inattention. Recall a time you 'knew' something but weren't paying attention—like a formula or a name. Did that knowledge exist in your mind? What does this tell you about the nature of your own knowledge and the need for conscious awareness?
?
If your mental form of an object is the same as its essence, differing only in mode of existence, then is your knowledge of Allah—gained through scripture and creation—truly a grasp of His essence or merely a representation? How does this lesson challenge you to deepen your understanding without overstepping the limits of human intellect?

Distinguishing Knowledge, Form, and Known Object
Distinguishes knowledge, form, and known object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between knowledge as the form itself, the known object as the bearer of the form, and how universality applies to the known object.

Core Idea
Knowledge can be considered as the mental form itself or as an additional concept; the known object is the bearer of the form.

This passage clarifies an important nuance in the definition of knowledge. Some scholars equate knowledge with the mental form, while others see it as an additional concept. Additionally, the known object (al-maʿlūm) is not the form itself but the entity that bears the form. This distinction allows us to understand how universality can be attributed to the known object through second intelligibles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the nature of knowledge as form
Read the Arabic text that discusses two perspectives on knowledge: one that sees knowledge as identical to the form, and another that sees it as an additional concept. Note the implications.
This reflects a philosophical debate on the nature of mental content.
2
Understand the concept of 'known object'
The passage states that the known object is the bearer of the form, not the form itself. Consider what that means for things like universality.
This prevents confusion between the mental image and the external thing.
3
Analyze the role of second intelligibles
The passage mentions that the form can later be subjected to intellection and acquire rulings called second intelligibles, such as universality. This is how we say, for example, that 'human' is a universal.
Second intelligibles are concepts like 'universal', 'particular', 'genus', 'species' that apply to mental concepts themselves.

Second Answer: Different Senses of 'Acquisition' (Husul)
Distinguishes 'acquisition' in knowledge from acquisition of an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributive acquisition (husul ittisafi) and perceptual acquisition (husul idraki), and why this distinction resolves the objection that the soul would be qualified by the attributes it perceives.

Core Idea
Not all 'acquisitions' are alike; perceptual acquisition does not imply being qualified.

This passage presents a second answer to the objection that if the soul perceives its attributes, it would be qualified by them. The author distinguishes between different meanings of 'acquisition', showing that the acquisition involved in perception does not necessitate the soul becoming qualified. This distinction is crucial for understanding how we can know our own states without being defined by them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The text ends abruptly; we will only cover the available portion.
2
Identify the multiple meanings of 'husul'
Notice the examples given: money/owner, blackness/body, speed/motion, form/matter, present/person. These illustrate the range of meanings.
3
Focus on the key distinction
The author distinguishes between attributive acquisition (implies qualification) and perceptual acquisition (does not imply qualification). Highlight this contrast.
Attributive acquisition pertains to accidents in a subject; perceptual acquisition pertains to presence or knowledge.
4
Understand the implication
Because perceptual acquisition does not entail qualification, the soul can perceive its own attributes (like faith) without becoming qualified by them. This refutes the objection.
5
Note the incomplete argument
The passage ends with a fragment about self-perception implying non-existence. This requires further context from subsequent pages.
The argument is not fully concluded here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جوب آخر، وهو أن حصول الشيء للشيء يقال لمعان متعددة كحصول المال لصاحبه وبالعكس وحصول السواد للجسم وبالعكس، وحصول السرعة للحركة، وحصول الصورة للمادة وبالعكس وحصول كل منهما للجسم وبالعكس، وحصول الحاضر لما حضر عنده وبالعكس ولزوم الاتصاف إنما هو في حصول العرض بمحله ولا كذلك حصول الحاضر لما حضر عنده وبالعكس وهو معلوم لنا بالوجدان ومتحقق كونه حصولاً لنا وإن لم نقدر على التعبير عن خصوصيته بغير كونه إدراكاً وعلماً أو شعوراً أو إحاطة بكنه الشيء، أو ما يجري مجرى هذه الاعتبارات ) وبهذا أعني لكون الحصول الإدراكي مغايراً لحصول العرض للمحل المستلزم للاتصاف لا يلزم من إدراك المعاني التي تكون من صفات النفس كالإيمان والكفر، والجود والبخل ونحو ذلك اتصاف النفس بها لانتفاء الحصول الاتصافي، فكيف يلزم ذلك فيما ليس من شأن النفس الاتصاف بها كالحرارة والاستداره ونحو ذلك وإنما الكلام في أن الحصول الاتصافي هل يستلزم الحصول ادراكي حتى يلزم دوام تعقل النفس لصفاتها على ما زعموا ثم إنهم لم يبينوا أن ذلك مبنى على أن مجرد الحصول الاتصافي كاف في الادراك النفسي صفاتها، أو على أنه مستلزم للحصول الإدراكي والحق أن الكل بوجود غير متأصل هـو الصورة، وما ذكروا من أنه لوكان كذلك لزم من إدراك النفس لذاتها عدم
Another answer: that the acquisition (ḥuṣūl) of a thing for a thing is used in multiple meanings, like the acquisition of money for its owner and vice versa, the acquisition of blackness for a body and vice versa, the acquisition of speed for motion, the acquisition of form for matter and vice versa, the acquisition of each of them for a body and vice versa, the acquisition of the present for that which is present with it and vice versa. The implication of being qualified (luzūm al-ittiṣāf) is only in the acquisition of an accident in its locus (maḥall), not like the acquisition of the present for that which is present with it and vice versa. And this is known to us by direct experience (wijdān) and is realized as an acquisition for us, even if we cannot express its particularity except as perception, knowledge, awareness, or comprehension of the essence of the thing, or what runs in the course of these considerations. And by this, I mean that because the perceptual acquisition (al-ḥuṣūl al-idrākī) is different from the acquisition of an accident in its locus which necessitates qualification, it does not follow from perceiving meanings that are attributes of the soul, like faith and disbelief, generosity and stinginess, and the like, that the soul is qualified by them, because the attributive acquisition (al-ḥuṣūl al-ittiṣāfī) is absent. So how could that follow for things that the soul is not accustomed to be qualified by, like heat, roundness, and the like? Rather, the discussion is whether the attributive acquisition necessitates the perceptual acquisition, such that it would require the soul to always intellect its attributes as they claimed. Then they did not clarify whether this is based on the mere sufficiency of attributive acquisition for the soul's perception of its attributes, or on its necessitation of perceptual acquisition. The truth is that all is by a non-fundamental existence, which is the form. And what they mentioned—that if it were so, then from the soul's perception of itself would follow non-existence
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents a second answer to the objection, based on a distinction between different types of 'acquisition' (ḥuṣūl). The author explains that 'acquisition of a thing for a thing' has multiple meanings: e.g., money for its owner, blackness for a body, speed for motion, form for matter, and the present for the person it is present with. The critical point is that the implication of being qualified (ittiṣāf) only applies to the acquisition of an accident in its subject (maḥall), not to the acquisition of something present (ḥāḍir) to a person. The latter is known by direct experience as a kind of acquisition that we call perception, knowledge, awareness, or comprehension. Since perceptual acquisition is different from attributive acquisition, it does not follow that when we perceive attributes of the soul (like faith, disbelief) the soul itself becomes qualified by them. Even more so for external qualities like heat and roundness, which are not attributes of the soul. The author then addresses a further point: does the attributive acquisition (of an accident) necessitate perceptual acquisition? Some claimed that if the soul has an attribute, it must always be aware of it. The author says the truth is that all is by a non-fundamental existence (wujūd ghayr mutaʾaṣṣil), which is the form. The text ends with a fragment about the soul's self-perception implying non-existence, which is incomplete.

Key Terms
حصول
حصول الاتصافي
حصول الإدراكي
اتصاف
عرض بمحله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of 'acquisition' (ḥuṣūl) are the same, so if the soul perceives an attribute, it must be qualified by it.
Correction: Perceptual acquisition (ḥuṣūl idrākī) does not imply qualification (ittiṣāf); only attributive acquisition (ḥuṣūl ittiṣāfī) does. Thus, the soul can perceive its attributes without being qualified by them.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer, what is the key difference between the acquisition involved in knowledge (al-ḥuṣūl al-idrākī) and the acquisition of an accident in its locus?
A) Knowledge acquisition implies qualification, while accident acquisition does not.
B) Accident acquisition implies qualification, while knowledge acquisition does not.
C) Both are identical in meaning.
D) Neither implies qualification.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between attributive and perceptual acquisition resolves the objection that perceiving attributes would qualify the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson teaches that perceiving a quality is not the same as being qualified by it. How can you separate your awareness of a fault from your identity, and what practical step will you take tonight to acknowledge a fault without letting it define you?
?
Think of someone you have judged harshly recently because you perceived a negative trait in them (e.g., stinginess). Did you assume that your perception meant they were fundamentally that way? According to this passage, perceptual acquisition does not entail qualification. How does this truth change your responsibility toward that person? Will you consciously withhold judgment and instead see them as complex?
?
You recite the shahada and feel faith in your heart. But is that feeling merely a perceptual acquisition (awareness of faith) or does it truly qualify your soul as faithful? The distinction in this lesson forces you to ask: what evidence in your daily actions shows that faith has become an attributive acquisition in your soul? What is one concrete change you will make this week to ensure your faith is not just perceived but possessed?

Two Arguments Against Identifying Knowledge with Sensation
Author gives two arguments against identifying knowledge with sensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key arguments from the Islamic theological tradition that refute the claim that knowledge and sensation are essentially the same.

Core Idea
Knowledge and sensation differ in essence, not just in origin.

This passage presents two concise arguments against equating knowledge (ʿilm) with sensation (iḥsās). The author first points out that knowledge can exist without sensation, and then clarifies that the debate becomes merely verbal once we admit they are distinct types of perception. These arguments are crucial for understanding the unique nature of knowledge in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get a sense of the author's argument structure.
Notice the two 'As for the first/second' markers.
2
Analyze the first argument
The first argument states that it is possible for something to be known by another knowledge while lacking sensation, proving knowledge is distinct from sensation.
The key point is the 'necessity' of possibility.
3
Analyze the second argument
The second argument shows that if we grant they are different kinds of perception, the dispute becomes about the definition of 'knowledge'—a verbal issue.
The author concludes that applying 'knowledge' to sensation is linguistically incorrect.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
The author asserts that knowledge and sensation are different in reality, not just in how we acquire them.
This refutes the claim that they are identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما أولا فلأن إمكان تعلق علم آخر به ضروري. كيف وأنا نحكم عليه عدم الإحساس أيضاً . وأما ثانياً : فلأن مقصود الجمهور نفي أن تكون حقيقة إدراك الشيء بالحس حقيقة إدراكه المسمى بالعلم ، بحيث لا تفاوت إلا في طريق الحصول كما في العلم بالشيء بطريق الاستدلال أو الإلهام، أو الحدس، وإما بعد تسليم كونهما نوعين مختلفين من الإدراك فيصير البحث لفظياً مبنياً على أن العلم اسم لمطلق الإدراك، أو النوع منه، والحق أن إطلاقه على الإحساس مخالف للعرف واللغة فإنه اسم لغيره من الإدراكات.
As for the first [argument]: because the possibility of another knowledge [sci. a knowledge distinct from sensation] being attached to it is necessary. How [can it be otherwise] while we also judge that it is lacking sensation? As for the second [argument]: because the aim of the majority [of scholars] is to negate that the reality of perceiving a thing by sense is the reality of perceiving it called knowledge, such that there is no difference except in the mode of attainment, as in knowledge of a thing by way of inference or inspiration or intuition. But if one concedes that they are two different kinds of perception, then the inquiry becomes verbal, founded on whether the name 'knowledge' applies to absolute perception or to a type thereof. The truth is that applying it [the term 'knowledge'] to sensation violates custom and language, for it [the term 'knowledge'] is a name for other perceptions.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the claim that knowledge ('ilm) and sensation (ihsas) are identical in reality, differing only in mode of acquisition. The author gives two arguments. First, it is necessarily possible for something to be known by another kind of knowledge, while we also judge that it lacks sensation—proving that knowledge and sensation are not the same. Second, the majority's intention is to deny that the essence of sense-perception is the same as the essence of knowledge, such that there would be no difference except in how the perception is obtained (e.g., through reasoning, inspiration, or intuition). If one grants that they are two different kinds of perception, the debate becomes merely verbal—whether 'knowledge' is a name for all perception or for a specific type. The truth, the author asserts, is that applying 'knowledge' to sensation contradicts common usage and language, for 'knowledge' properly refers to other forms of perception.

إحساس
إدراك
استدلال
إلهام
حدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge and sensation differ only in origin, but the author argues they differ in essence.
Correction: The passage shows that knowledge can be present without sensation, and that equating them leads to a verbal dispute, indicating a real essential difference.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what makes the debate about knowledge and sensation merely verbal if one admits they are different kinds of perception?
A) It becomes a dispute over the definition of 'knowledge'.
B) The majority's view is already accepted.
C) Both are essentially the same.
D) Sensation is a type of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and sensation are distinct in essence, and equating them leads to a verbal dispute or logical impossibility.
