Mu'tazili Views on Will and the Ash'ari Response
Al-Taftazani presents Mu'tazili views on will and the Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili definitions of will (as belief in benefit or inclination) and the key Ash'ari counter-argument that refines the definition.

Core Idea
Will is not mere belief in benefit; it must be directed toward achievable good.

The Mu'tazila sought to define human will in naturalistic terms, reducing it to cognitive states. In this passage, al-Taftazani presents their two main theories—will as belief in benefit, and will as inclination—along with the decisive Ash'ari response. This refutation clarifies the Islamic understanding of will as requiring both knowledge of good and the ability to attain it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first view, second view, answer.
2
Identify the two Mu'tazili definitions
The passage presents two views: many Mu'tazila said will is belief in benefit or its presumption; some said it is an inclination following that belief. Find these in the text.
3
Understand their reasoning
For the first view, the reasoning is that ability is equally related to both sides of an action, so belief in benefit tips the scale. For the second, they observed that one may believe in benefit but not will until an inclination arises.
4
Analyze the answer
The answer has two parts: it refines the definition of belief in benefit to include achievability without hindrance, and it critiques the notion of inclination as applying only to the powerless, not the fully capable agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب كثير من المعتزلة إلى أن الإرادة اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه، فإن نسبة القدرة إلى طرفي الفعل على السوية فإذا حصل في القلب اعتقاد النفع في أحد طرفيه، أو ظنه ترجح بسببه ذلك الطرف وصار مؤثراً عنده. وذهب بعضهم إلى أنها ميل يعقب اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه، لأن القادر كثيراً ما يعتقد النفع أو يظنه ولا يريده ما لم يحدث هذا الميل. وأجيب بأنا لا نجعله مجرد اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه، بل اعتقاد نفع له أو إلى غيره ممن يؤثر خيره، بحيث يمكن وصول ذلك النفع إليه، أو إلى غيره من غير مانع من تعب أو معارضة، وما ذكر من الميل إنما يحصل لمن لا يقدر على تحصيل ذلك الشيء قدرة تامة كالشوق إلى المحبوب لمن لا يصل إليه، أما في القادر التام القدرة فيكفي الاعتقاد المذكور.
And many of the Mu'tazila held that will is belief in benefit or its presumption. For the relation of ability to the two sides of the action is equal. So when belief in benefit in one of its two sides, or its presumption, occurs in the heart, that side becomes preponderant by it and becomes effective for him. And some of them held that it is an inclination that follows belief in benefit or its presumption, because the capable one often believes in benefit or presumes it but does not will it until this inclination occurs. And it was answered: we do not make it mere belief in benefit or its presumption, but rather belief in benefit for him or for another whom he wishes good for, such that the arrival of that benefit to him or to another is possible without any impediment of fatigue or opposition. And what was mentioned of inclination only occurs for one who is not able to obtain that thing with complete ability, like longing for a beloved for one who cannot reach him. As for the one with complete ability, the mentioned belief suffices.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents and responds to the Mu'tazili views on will (al-irāda). First, it states that many Mu'tazila held that will is belief in benefit (iʿtiqād al-nafʿ) or its presumption (ẓann). Their reasoning: since ability (al-qudra) is equally related to both sides of an action, when belief in benefit (or presumption of benefit) occurs in the heart regarding one side, that side becomes preponderant and thus the agent wills it. Second, some Mu'tazila held that will is an inclination (mayl) that follows belief or presumption of benefit, because a capable person often believes or presumes benefit but does not will until this inclination arises. The author then gives the answer (wa-ujība): will is not merely belief in benefit or its presumption; rather it is belief in benefit that can reach the agent or another whom he wishes good for, without any hindrance like fatigue or opposition. The inclination mentioned (the desire-like longing) only occurs for one who is not fully capable of obtaining that thing, like longing for a beloved one cannot reach. But for one with complete ability (al-qādir al-tāmm al-qudra), the mentioned belief is sufficient. This answer refines the Mu'tazili definition and prepares for the Ash'ari view.

Key Terms
الإرادة
اعتقاد النفع
ظن النفع
الميل
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazili definitions of will (as belief in benefit or inclination) are correct and complete.
Correction: The answer shows that mere belief in benefit is insufficient; it must be belief in benefit that is achievable without hindrance. Inclination is only relevant for those lacking complete ability, not for the fully capable agent.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given, what additional condition must be present for belief in benefit to constitute will?
A) The belief must be certain
B) The benefit must be achievable without hindrance
C) An inclination must also be present
D) The agent must be incapable

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view refines will as belief in achievable benefit, not mere inclination or presumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you set out to do something, do you honestly examine whether the benefit you expect is truly reachable without great difficulty, or do you often act on mere assumption? Will you commit to pausing before your next big decision to assess the real possibility of achieving the good you intend?
?
The Ash'ari answer says will includes wanting benefit for others, not just yourself. In your daily life, whose benefit are you actually seeking? Is there someone you claim to care for but your actions show otherwise? Confront the gap and resolve to realign your will.
?
The source says inclination and longing only occur for those who lack complete ability to attain what they desire. Where in your life do you feel a deep longing? What does that tell you about the areas where you feel powerless? How can this lesson help you trust your God-given capacity to act on clear beliefs, rather than remain stuck in desire?

Power Before the Act: Refuting Two Arguments
Sanusi refutes two arguments against power preceding the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to refute arguments that claim an act cannot be within power before its occurrence, and the distinction between conditional and unconditional possibility.

Core Idea
Impossibility at a given moment does not negate potential power; it only requires a change in conditions.

Sanusi presents two arguments by those who deny that power can precede an act. The first invokes the impossibility of an act while nonexistent; the second poses a logical contradiction. Understanding his refutation requires grasping the difference between conditional and unconditional possibility. This lesson unpacks these arguments and their answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: two arguments followed by a common answer.
The answer begins with a refutation by eternal power.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the second and third arguments. The second argues impossibility from coexistence of existence and non-existence; the third argues a contradiction if power precedes the act.
3
Analyze the answer's structure
The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'impossible': conditional impossibility under a description vs. impossibility in the specific time itself. Understand how this refutes both arguments.
Use the analogy of Zayd's standing while sitting.
4
Apply the distinction
Explain why the act is conditionally impossible but not absolutely impossible, and thus still within power.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا يخفى أن الكلام إلزامي على من يقول بتأثير القدرة الحادثة . الثاني : أن الفعل حال عدمه ممتنع لاستحالة اجتماع الوجود والعدم ولا شيء من الممتنع بمقدور وفاقاً. الثالث : لو كانت القدرة قبل الفعل لكان الفعل قبل وقوعه ممكناً لكنه محال لأنه يلزم من فرض وقوعه كون القدرة معه لا قبله هذا خلف . والوجهان متقاربان وجوابهما بعد النقض بالقدرة القديمة إنه إن أريد بامتناع الفعل حال العدم وقيل الحدوث امتناعه مع وصف كونه معدومة وغير واقع فممنوع لكنه لا ينافي المقدورية فإمكان الحصول من القادر وإن أريد امتناعه في زمان عدمه وكونه غير واقع فممنوع بل هو ممكن بأن يحصل بدل عدمه الوجود كما هو شأن سائر الممكنات وهذا كقيام زيد فإنه ممتنع مع القعود وبشرطه لكنه ممكن حال القعود، وفي زمانه بأن يزول القعود ويحصل القيام .
Then it is not hidden that the discourse is obligatory upon those who say that the originated power has an effect. The second [argument]: the act at the time of its nonexistence is impossible due to the impossibility of combining existence and nonexistence, and nothing of the impossible is within power, by agreement. The third [argument]: if power were before the act, then the act before its occurrence would be possible, but it is impossible because from the supposition of its occurrence, it follows that power is with it, not before it; this is a contradiction. The two aspects are close, and their answer, after refuting by the eternal power, is that if by the impossibility of the act at the time of nonexistence — and it is said [at the time of] coming-to-be — is meant its impossibility with the description of being nonexistent and not occurring, then it is prevented, but it does not negate potentiality [of being within power], for possibility of obtaining from the powerful [agent remains]. And if by it is meant its impossibility in the time of its nonexistence and its being not occurring, then it is prevented; rather, it is possible by [the fact that] existence replaces its nonexistence, as is the case of all possibilities. And this is like Zayd's standing: it is impossible with sitting and under its condition, but it is possible in the state of sitting and in its time by [the fact that] sitting ceases and standing occurs.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage refutes two arguments used by those who deny that power can be before the act. The author first presents the second argument: the act at the time of its non-existence is impossible because existence and non-existence cannot coexist, and what is impossible is not within power. The third argument: if power existed before the act, the act would be possible before its occurrence, but that is impossible because if it were to occur, power would be with it, not before—a contradiction. The author then gives the answer by first invoking the eternal power as a refutation. He explains that if by 'impossible at the time of non-existence' one means the act is impossible while it is described as nonexistent and not happening, that is conceded but does not negate its being potentially within power, because it is still possible for the powerful agent to bring it about. If one means it is impossible in that specific time slot, that is denied: it is possible for existence to replace non-existence, just as other possibilities. The analogy of Zayd's standing while he is sitting illustrates this: standing is impossible under the condition of sitting, but it is possible in the state of sitting because sitting can cease and standing can occur. Thus, an act's non-existence in a given moment does not make it impossible for it to be within power.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة
المقدورية
الامتناع
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an act is impossible at a given time, it cannot be within power at all.
Correction: The act is conditionally impossible (due to present state) but unconditionally possible (the state can change, so power can effect it).

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to show that an act can be possible even if it is impossible at a given moment?
A) The creation of the world
B) Zayd's standing while sitting
C) The eternal power of God
D) The combination of existence and non-existence

Key Takeaway
An act can be impossible at a given moment due to coexisting conditions but still be within power, because conditions can change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim you can't stop a sin because you've been sinning for years—does Sanusi's distinction between impossibility under a condition and absolute impossibility expose your excuse as a self-deception? What will you do today to prove that your power can precede change even in the midst of your habit?
?
When you think about a missed prayer you haven't made up, do you tell yourself it's impossible to pray now because the time has passed? How does Zayd's standing analogy challenge the way you ration your obedience?
?
If you truly believed that power can precede the act, which good deed have you been postponing because you thought you were 'not ready'? What is stopping you from intending it right now, even if conditions seem unfavorable?

Inability: Opposite of Power, Not Mere Absence
Introduces the definition and initial disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the majority of theologians consider inability a real attribute opposed to power, rather than a mere absence of capacity.

Core Idea
Inability is a real quality, not just missing power.

Theologians debate whether inability is something positive or just a lack. Abu Hashim argued it is only the absence of the capacity to act. The majority, however, maintain that inability is a genuine attribute that opposes power. This passage introduces the key distinction between the incapacitated and the prevented, and begins the argument against Abu Hashim.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the definition of inability and the immediate rejection of Abu Hashim's view.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two views
Identify the view of Abu Hashim (inability as absence of capacity) and the initial stance of the Jumhur (inability as opposite of power). Note the difference between the incapacitated (zaman) and the prevented (mamnu‘).
3
Understand the branch argument
The passage mentions a transmitted opinion about the object of inability being the existing thing. See how this is refuted with the example of the challengers (mutahaddin).
This branch reinforces the argument that inability is not a mere absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث العجز ضد القدرة لا عدم ملكة كما هو رأي أبي هاشم لما نجد من ذلك الفرق بين الزمن والممنوع مع اشتراكهما في عدم القدرة وله أن يمنع في الممنوع أو يجعل الفرق أن من شأنه القدرة بخلاف الزمن ويتفرع عن التضاد ما نقل عن الشيخ وإن كان خلاف الظاهر أن متعلق العجز هو الموجود حتى إن الزمن عاجز عن القعود بمعنى أن فيه صفة تستعقب القعود لا عن قدرة ويبطله القطع بأن عجز المتحدين إنما هو عن الإتيان بمثل القرآن والتزام اشتراك اللفظ بين تلك الصفة وعدم القدرة خلاف اللغة الجمهور على أن العجز عرض ثابت مضاد للقدرة للقطع بأن في الزمن معنى لا يوجد في الممنوع مع اشتراكهما في عدم التمكن من الفعل وعند أبي هاشم هو عدم ملكة للقدرة وليس في الزمن صفة متحققة تضاد القدرة بل الفرق أن الزمن ليس بقادر والممنوع قادر بالفعل أو من شأنه القدرة بطريق جري العادة على ما سبق
The third section: Inability is the opposite of power, not the absence of the capacity to act as is the opinion of Abu Hashim, because we find from that the difference between the incapacitated and the prevented despite their sharing in the lack of power; and he can deny in the case of the prevented, or make the difference that the incapacitated is one who normally has power unlike the prevented. And from the opposition there branches what is transmitted from the Shaykh, though contrary to the apparent meaning, that the object of inability is the existing thing so that the incapacitated is unable to sit in the sense that he possesses a quality that necessitates sitting but not from power; and it is invalidated by the certainty that the inability of the challengers is only about bringing the like of the Quran, and insisting on the homonymy of the word between that quality and the lack of power is contrary to linguistic usage. The majority hold that inability is a fixed attribute opposing power, due to the certainty that in the incapacitated there is a meaning not found in the prevented with their sharing in the lack of ability to act. And according to Abu Hashim, it is the absence of the capacity for power, and there is no actual quality in the incapacitated opposing power; rather the difference is that the incapacitated is not powerful while the prevented is actually powerful, or is usually capable as was mentioned.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage defines 'ajz (inability) and presents the main disagreement between the Jumhur (majority) and Abu Hashim al-Jubbai (a Mu'tazili). The author begins by stating the definition: inability is the opposite of qudra (power). He rejects Abu Hashim's view that inability is merely 'adam milkat al-qudra (the absence of the capacity for power). The argument for the majority view is based on the observed distinction between the zaman (incapacitated person, e.g., a feeble old man) and the mamnu' (one who is prevented from acting, e.g., by external constraint). Both share in the lack of actual ability to perform a specified action, but the zaman is not considered powerful, while the mamnu' is still regarded as powerful in potential. This shows that in the zaman there is an actual attribute (sifa) that opposes power. The passage then branches into a discussion attributed to al-Ash'ari: the object (muta'allaq) of inability is the existing thing (mawjud), so the zaman is unable to sit in the sense that he possesses a quality that necessitates sitting but not from power. This is refuted by the example of the challengers (mutahaddin) who aim to produce the like of the Quran: they are unable to bring it, not to do something else. If inability only related to existing things, they would have to be unable to bring something non-existing—a contradiction. Finally, Abu Hashim's exact position is restated: inability is the mere absence of the capacity for power, with no actual quality in the incapacitated; the difference between the zaman and mamnu' is that the latter is actually powerful or normally capable, while the former is not. The author clearly sides with the majority view that inability is a real attribute opposing power.

Key Terms
العجز
القدرة
الزمن
الممنوع
عدم الملكة
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inability is merely the absence of power, so the incapacitated and prevented are the same.
Correction: The passage shows a clear difference: the incapacitated has a quality opposing power, while the prevented only lacks the ability to act.

Checkpoint
According to the first part of the passage, what distinguishes the incapacitated (zaman) from the prevented (mamnu')?
A) The incapacitated is actually powerful, the prevented is not.
B) The incapacitated has an attribute opposing power, while the prevented has a capacity that is merely blocked.
C) Both are equally unable, so there is no difference.
D) The incapacitated lacks the capacity for power, the prevented has the capacity but cannot act.

Key Takeaway
Inability is a real attribute opposed to power, as shown by the distinction between the incapacitated and the prevented.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If inability is a real quality that opposes power, how do you normally justify your own 'I can't'? Are you honest about whether it is genuine inability or an excuse? This lesson forces you to examine the excuses you give yourself for not fulfilling obligations.
?
The challengers were unable to produce the like of the Quran, and their inability was real. When you claim you cannot do something difficult, are you acknowledging a real limitation or hiding from responsibility? Let this lesson hold you accountable for honestly distinguishing between incapacity and lack of effort.

The Majority View: Inability as a Fixed Attribute
Restates the Jumhur view and clarifies Abu Hashim's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority position on inability and how it differs from Abu Hashim's final formulation.

Core Idea
The majority see inability as an actual quality; Abu Hashim sees it as mere absence.

The passage now restates the majority position clearly: inability is a fixed attribute that opposes power. It also clarifies Abu Hashim's view, which holds that no real quality exists in the incapacitated, only a lack of power. The distinction remains that the incapacitated is not powerful while the prevented is powerful or normally capable.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the emphatic statement of the Jumhur position.
2
Analyze the Jumhur proof
The Jumhur prove their view by pointing to the meaning in the incapacitated that is absent in the prevented. Understand why this indicates a real attribute.
The proof is based on certainty (qat‘).
3
Compare with Abu Hashim's refined view
Abu Hashim's view is restated: inability is absence of capacity, but the difference between the two cases is that the prevented is actually powerful or normally capable, while the incapacitated is not.
Note that Abu Hashim still denies a real attribute.

?

The Basic Definition of Pleasure and Pain
Al-Taftazani defines pleasure and pain with the condition of 'insofar as it is agreeable/disagreeable.'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the standard kalam definition of pleasure and pain, including the crucial restriction 'insofar as it is agreeable/disagreeable.'

Core Idea
Pleasure is not just any perception of something agreeable; it must be perceived specifically as agreeable.

In this passage, al-Taftazani begins by clarifying that pleasure and pain are types of perception with a special relation to the essence of the agreeable or disagreeable. He then presents the standard definitions that dominate Islamic theology. The key is the phrase 'insofar as it is agreeable,' which prevents misapplications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the repeated emphasis on the qualifier 'insofar as'.
2
Focus on the restriction 'insofar as'
Pleasure is defined as perception of the agreeable specifically insofar as it is agreeable. Why is this necessary?
Consider the example of the jaundiced person who does not take pleasure in sweetness.
3
Understand the role of the restriction
The restriction excludes cases where a thing is agreeable in one respect but not perceived from that aspect. The example of the sick person repelled by medicine further illustrates this.
This condition ensures that only perception from the agreeable aspect counts as pleasure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الملائم والمنافي من حيث هما كذلك فهما نوعان من الإدراك اعتبر فيهما إضافة تختلف بالقياس إلى المدرك وإضافة لذات الملائم أو المنافي لا صورتهما والحق أنا نجد حالة هي اللذة، ونعلم أن ثمة إدراكاً للملائم واما انهما نفسه، أو أمر حاصل به . وهل يحصل بغيره، ففيه تردد. وقد يفسر بالخروج عن الحالة الغير الطبيعية ويبطله الالتذاذ بإصابة مال، أو مطالعة جمال من غير طلب وشوق . أي من الكيفيات النفسانية اللذة والألم وتصورهما بديهي كسائر الوجدانيات، وقد يفسران قصداً إلى تعيين المسمى وتلخيصه فيقال: اللذة ، إدراك الملائم من حيث هو ملائم، والألم إدراك المنافي من حيث هو مناف والملائم للشيء كماله الخاص أعني الأمر اللائق به كالتكيف بالحلاوة للذائقة، وتعقل الأشياء على ما هي عليه للعاقلة، وقيد بالحيثية لأن الشيء قد يكون ملائماً من وجه دون وجه فإدراكه لا من جهة الملائمة لا يكون لذة كالصفراوي لا يلتذ بالحلو، والمراد بالإدراك الوصول إلى ذات الملائم لا إلى مجرد صورته، فان تخيل اللذيذ غير اللذة ولذا كان الأقرب ما قال ابن سينا : إن اللذة إدراك ونيل لوصول ما هو عند المدرك كمال وخير من حيث هو كذلك ، فذكر مع الإدراك النيل. أعني الإصابة والوجدان، لأن إدراك الشيء قد يكون بحصول صورة تساويه، ونيله لا يكون إلا بحصول ذاته، واللذة لا تتم بحصول ما يساوي اللذيذ، بل إنما يتم بحصول ذاته. وذكر إدراك حصول اللذيذ للملتذ، ووصوله إليه والفرق بين الكمال والخير هو أن حصول ما يناسب الشيء ويليق به من حيث اقتضائه براءة ما لذلك الشيء من القوة إلى الفعل كمال له ومن حيث كونه مؤثراً خير ، ثم المعتبر كماليته وخيريته بالقياس إلى المدرك لا في نفس الأمر لأنه قد يعتقد الكمالية والخيرية في شيء فيستلذ به، وإن لم يكونا فيه وقد لا يعتقدهما فيما تحققنا فيه فلا يلتذ به، ولهذا يحصل من شيء معين لذة، أو ألم لزيد دون عمرو وبالعكس، فكل من اللذة والألم نوع من الإدراك اعتبر فيه إضافة إلى ملائم، أو مناف يختلف بالقياس إلى المدرك، وإصابة ووجدان لذات الملائم، أو المنافي من حيث هو كذلك لا للصورة الحاصلة منه، وبقيد الحيثية يندفع ما يقال : إن المريض قد يلتذ بالحلاوة مع أنها لا تلائمه بل تضره وينفر عن الأدوية، وهي تلائمه وتنفعه، وذكر الإمام بعد الاعتراف بأن اللذة والألم حقيقتان غنيتان عن التعريف أنا نجد من أنفسنا حالة نسميها باللذة ونعرف أن هناك إدراكاً للملائم لكن لم يثبت لنا أن اللذة نفس إدراك الملائم أو غيره، وبتقدير المغايرة هل هي معلولة له أم لا؟ وبتقدير المعلولية هل يمكن حصولها بطريق آخر؟
The agreeable and the disagreeable, as such, are two types of perception in which a relation is considered that differs relative to the perceiver, and a relation to the essence of the agreeable or disagreeable, not to their form. The truth is that we find a state that is pleasure, and we know that there is a perception of the agreeable, but whether they are identical or pleasure is something obtained by it? And whether it can be obtained by something else? There is hesitation in that. It has been interpreted as departure from the unnatural state, but this is invalidated by the pleasure of obtaining wealth or viewing beauty without desire or longing. That is, among the psychic qualities are pleasure and pain, and their conception is intuitive like all found things. They may be interpreted with the aim of specifying the named and summarizing it, so it is said: pleasure is the perception of the agreeable insofar as it is agreeable, and pain is the perception of the disagreeable insofar as it is disagreeable. The agreeable for a thing is its specific perfection, i.e., the thing suitable to it, like being qualified by sweetness for the taste faculty, and the intellection of things as they are for the rational faculty. The restriction 'insofar as' is because a thing may be agreeable in one respect but not another, so its perception not from the aspect of agreeableness is not pleasure, like a jaundiced person does not take pleasure in something sweet. What is meant by perception is reaching the essence of the agreeable, not merely its image, for the imagination of the delicious is not the pleasure. Hence the nearest is what Ibn Sina said: Pleasure is perception and attainment of the arrival of what is, relative to the perceiver, perfection and good insofar as it is so. He mentioned attainment along with perception, meaning finding and experiencing, because perception of a thing may be by the occurrence of an image equal to it, while its attainment is only by the occurrence of its essence, and pleasure is not completed by the occurrence of what is equal to the delicious, but only by the occurrence of its essence. He mentioned the perception of the arrival of the delicious to the pleasure-taker and its reaching him. The difference between perfection and good is that the occurrence of what is suitable and fitting for a thing, by virtue of its necessitating the emergence of that thing from potentiality to actuality, is perfection for it, and by virtue of its being effective, it is good. Then what is considered is its perfection and goodness relative to the perceiver, not in themselves, because one may believe perfection and goodness in a thing and thus take pleasure in it even if they are not in it, and may not believe them in something in which they are verified and thus not take pleasure in it. Hence, a particular thing may produce pleasure or pain for Zayd but not for Amr, and vice versa. So each of pleasure and pain is a type of perception in which is considered a relation to an agreeable or disagreeable that differs relative to the perceiver, and an attainment and finding of the essence of the agreeable or disagreeable insofar as it is so, not of the form obtained from it. By the restriction of 'insofar as' is repelled what is said: that a sick person may take pleasure in sweetness even though it is not agreeable to him but harms him, and recoil from medicines that are agreeable and benefit him. And the Imam mentioned, after acknowledging that pleasure and pain are realities independent of definition, that we find in ourselves a state we call pleasure and know that there is a perception of the agreeable, but it has not been established for us whether pleasure is identical to the perception of the agreeable or something else; and assuming difference, is it caused by it or not? And assuming causality, can it be obtained by another path?
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage defines pleasure and pain and examines their nature in detail. The author begins by stating that the agreeable and disagreeable are two types of perception, but with a relation to the essence, not the form. He affirms that we directly experience pleasure, but questions whether pleasure is identical to the perception of the agreeable or something obtained through it. He then gives the standard definitions: pleasure is perception of the agreeable insofar as it is agreeable, pain is perception of the disagreeable insofar as it is disagreeable. The 'insofar as' condition is crucial because a thing may be agreeable in one aspect but not another; only the perception from the agreeable aspect constitutes pleasure. The author clarifies that perception here means reaching the essence, not just an image, since imagining the delicious is not the same as tasting it. This leads to Ibn Sina's refinement, which adds 'attainment' (nayl) to perception, because pleasure requires the actual arrival of the agreeable thing to the perceiver. The author then explains the difference between perfection and good: perfection is the actualization of potential, while good is the effective aspect. Importantly, these are considered relative to the perceiver, not absolutely, because one can take pleasure in something mistakenly believed to be perfect or good. This relativity explains why the same thing can give pleasure to one person and pain to another. Finally, the author reports the Imam's (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) epistemological caution: pleasure and pain are known directly, but their relationship to perception is not definitively settled—whether they are the same as perception, or caused by it, or obtainable otherwise, remains open.

Key Terms
اللذة
الألم
الملائم
المنافي
الإدراك
الحيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is simply the perception of something agreeable, regardless of the aspect.
Correction: Pleasure requires perception of the agreeable specifically as agreeable; a thing may be agreeable in one respect but not another.

Checkpoint
Why does the author include the phrase 'insofar as it is agreeable' in the definition of pleasure?
A) To indicate that pleasure is subjective.
B) To exclude cases where a thing is agreeable in one aspect but not perceived from that aspect.
C) To emphasize the role of imagination.
D) To distinguish between sensory and intellectual pleasure.

Key Takeaway
Pleasure is perception of the agreeable specifically as agreeable, not just any perception of an agreeable thing.

Ibn Sina's Refinement and Epistemological Caution
Al-Taftazani presents Ibn Sina's definition and al-Razi's cautious stance on the nature of pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina added 'attainment' to the definition of pleasure and why the relationship between pleasure and perception remains epistemologically uncertain.

Core Idea
Pleasure requires the actual arrival of the agreeable thing, not just its mental image.

After presenting the standard definition, al-Taftazani turns to Ibn Sina's more precise formulation, which adds the concept of attainment. He also reports Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's caution that while we directly experience pleasure, its exact nature is not fully settled.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's definition and explanation
Read the passage where Ibn Sina adds 'attainment' to perception. Why is perception alone insufficient?
Focus on the distinction between having an image and having the actual thing.
2
Understand perfection and good as relative
Ibn Sina mentions that what is perceived as perfection and good is relative to the perceiver. How does this explain individual differences in pleasure?
The example of Zayd and Amr having different reactions to the same thing.
3
Reflect on al-Razi's epistemic humility
Al-Razi notes that we directly experience pleasure but don't know its exact relation to perception. Why is this caution important?
It keeps the definition open to further investigation.

الملائم والمنافي من حيث هما كذلك فهما نوعان من الإدراك اعتبر فيهما إضافة تختلف بالقياس إلى المدرك وإضافة لذات الملائم أو المنافي لا صورتهما والحق أنا نجد حالة هي اللذة، ونعلم أن ثمة إدراكاً للملائم واما انهما نفسه، أو أمر حاصل به . وهل يحصل بغيره، ففيه تردد. وقد يفسر بالخروج عن الحالة الغير الطبيعية ويبطله الالتذاذ بإصابة مال، أو مطالعة جمال من غير طلب وشوق . أي من الكيفيات النفسانية اللذة والألم وتصورهما بديهي كسائر الوجدانيات، وقد يفسران قصداً إلى تعيين المسمى وتلخيصه فيقال: اللذة ، إدراك الملائم من حيث هو ملائم، والألم إدراك المنافي من حيث هو مناف والملائم للشيء كماله الخاص أعني بالقياس إلى المدرك لا في نفس الأمر لأنه قد يعتقد الكمالية والخيرية في شيء فيستلذ به، وإن لم يكونا فيه وقد لا يعتقدهما فيما تحققنا فيه فلا يلتذ به، ولهذا يحصل من شيء معين لذة، أو ألم لزيد دون عمرو وبالعكس، فكل من اللذة والألم نوع من الإدراك اعتبر فيه إضافة إلى ملائم، أو مناف يختلف بالقياس إلى المدرك، وإصابة ووجدان لذات الملائم، أو المنافي من حيث هو كذلك لا للصورة الحاصلة منه، وبقيد الحيثية يندفع ما يقال : إن المريض قد يلتذ بالحلاوة مع أنها لا تلائمه بل تضره وينفر عن الأدوية، وهي تلائمه وتنفعه، وذكر الإمام بعد الاعتراف بأن اللذة والألم حقيقتان غنيتان عن التعريف أنا نجد من أنفسنا حالة How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life have you settled for imagining spiritual growth—like memorizing a definition of pleasure—without actually pursuing the real attainment of nearness to Allah? What is one step you will take today to move from perception to attainment?
?
You believe you want Allah's pleasure, but is that desire genuine? Or do you merely find comfort in the idea of His mercy while avoiding the hard work of actual obedience? When will you stop imagining and start attaining?
?

Pain, Pleasure, and the Refutation of the Transition Theory
The author defines pain, presents the transition theory of pleasure, and refutes it with Ibn Sina's arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pleasure cannot be defined simply as transition from an unnatural to a natural state, and the role of perception and passivity in genuine pleasure and pain.

Core Idea
Pleasure is not mere transition; it requires perception through passivity.

This lesson examines the nature of pain and pleasure, engaging with the famous theory that pleasure is nothing but a transition back to a natural state. The author, following Ibn Sina, demonstrates why this view is inadequate, revealing deeper principles about the relationship between perception, passivity, and the essence of affective states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's careful argumentative structure.
The passage is dense with philosophical terminology.
2
Identify the author's initial claim about pain
The author states that pain is neither identical to the perception of what is harmful nor sufficient for pain. Note the medical example of moist imbalance.
This sets the stage for the discussion of pleasure.
3
Understand the transition theory of pleasure
Muhammad ibn Zakariyya (al-Razi) and Galen define pleasure as the change from an unnatural to a natural state. Recognise the examples given.
This theory is intuitive but flawed.
4
Analyse Ibn Sina's refutation
Ibn Sina provides two counterexamples: pleasure without prior pain/unnatural state (e.g., encountering a friend unexpectedly) and transition without pleasure (e.g., gradual recovery). Understand how these refute the definition.
Focus on the logic: the transition is neither necessary nor sufficient for pleasure.
5
Grasp the key insight: accident vs essence
The author concludes that the error lies in mistaking an accidental property (the occurrence of change) for the essence of pleasure. Reflect on why perception and passivity are essential.
This connects to broader kalam principles about essential vs. accidental properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقرب أن الألم ليس هو نفس إدراك المنافي ولا هو كاف في حصوله، لأن التجارب الطبية قد شهدت له بأن سوء المزاج الرطب غير مؤلم مع أن هناك إدراك أمر غير طبيعي، وسنتكلم على ذلك، وزعم محمد ابن زكريا أن اللذة عبارة عن التبدل والخروج عن حالة غير طبيعية إلى حالة طبيعية، وبه صرح جالينوس في مواضع من كلامه، وهو معنى الخلاص عن الألم، وذلك كالأكل للجوع، والجماع لدغدغة المنى أوعيته.
وأبطله ابن سينا وغيره بأنه قد تحصل اللذة من غير سابقة ألم، وحالة غير طبيعية كما في مصادفة حال ومطالعة جمال من غير طلب وشوق لا على التفصيل ولا على الإجمال بأن لم يخطر ذلك بباله قط لا جزئياً ولا كلياً، وكذا في إدراك الذائقة الحلاوة أول مرة، وقد يحصل ذلك التبدل من غير لذة كما في حصول الصحة على التدريج وفي ورود المستلذات من الطعوم والروائح والأصوات وغيرها على من له غاية الشوق إلى ذلك، وقد عرض له شاغل عن الشعور والإدراك قالوا : وسبب السهو أخذ ما بالعرض مكان ما بالذات، فان الألم واللذة لا يتمان إلا بإدراك، والإدراك الحسي خصوصاً اللمسي لا يحصل إلا بانفعال عن العند، ولذلك متى استقرت الكيفية الموجبة لذلك لم يحصل الانفعال فلم يحصل الإدراك بل تحصل لذة ولا ألم.
وبالجملة فلما لم تحصل اللذة إلا عند تبدل الحالة الغير الطبيعية ظنوا أنها نفسه ولا خفاء في إمكان معارضة هذا الكلام بالمثل، ودفعها بما سبق من الوجهين.
The closest [view] is that pain is not identical to the perception of the harmful (al-munāfī) nor sufficient for its occurrence, because medical experiments have testified for it that moist imbalance is not painful, although there is perception of an unnatural thing, and we will speak on that. And Muhammad ibn Zakariyya claimed that pleasure (al-ladhdha) is the change and exit from an unnatural state to a natural state, and Galen explicitly stated this in places of his speech, and it is the meaning of relief from pain, like eating for hunger, and intercourse for the tickling of the vessels of semen. And Ibn Sina and others refuted it by [saying] that pleasure may occur without prior pain and unnatural state, as in encountering a friend and viewing beauty without request and desire, neither in detail nor in summary, such that it had never crossed his mind at all, partially or wholly; likewise in the first tasting of sweetness by the tongue. And that change may occur without pleasure, as in gradual attainment of health and in the arrival of pleasurable things of tastes, smells, sounds, etc. to one who has extreme longing for it, but a distraction from sensation and perception occurred. They said: the cause of the oversight is taking the accidental in place of the essential. For pain and pleasure are not complete except with perception, and sensory perception, especially tactile, does not occur except through passivity (infiʿāl) from contact. Therefore, when the quality causing that is stable, passivity does not occur, so perception does not occur, but pleasure and pain do not occur. In sum, since pleasure does not occur except upon change from the unnatural state, they thought it is that [change] itself, and there is no concealment of the possibility of opposing this statement with a similar one and refuting it by the two aspects mentioned before.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of pain and pleasure, containing a definition, a false view, and a refutation. The author begins by stating the closest correct view: pain is not identical to the perception of what is harmful, nor is that perception sufficient for pain to occur. He supports this with medical evidence that a moist imbalance (sūʾ al-mizāj al-ratb) is not painful even though there is perception of an unnatural state. Then he presents the claim of Muhammad ibn Zakariyya (al-Razi) that pleasure is the transition from an unnatural state to a natural one, citing Galen and giving examples like eating when hungry or sexual intercourse when semen is about to be emitted. The author then reports Ibn Sina's refutation: pleasure can occur without prior pain or unnatural state, for example encountering a friend unexpectedly or seeing beauty without any prior desire or thought. The transition can also occur without pleasure, as in gradual recovery from illness, or when pleasurable things are presented to someone intensely longing for them but distracted. The author notes that the error lies in mistaking an accidental property (the occurrence of change) for the essence of pleasure. He explains that both pain and pleasure require perception, and sensory perception, especially tactile, requires passivity (infiʿāl) from contact with an opposing quality. When the quality causing the perception becomes stable, passivity ceases and perception ceases, so neither pleasure nor pain occurs. The passage concludes that because pleasure only occurs upon change, people mistakenly think it is the change itself, but this is refutable by counterexamples and the two aspects mentioned (presumably the requirements of perception and passivity). The commentary proceeds in the author's order, clarifying each point.

Key Terms
الألم (al-alam): pain
اللذة (al-ladhdha): pleasure
التبدل (al-tabaddu): change/transition
الانفعال (al-infiʿāl): passivity/receptivity
ما بالعرض (mā bi-l-ʿaraḍ): accidental property
ما بالذات (mā bi-l-dhāt): essential property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That pleasure is simply the transition from an unnatural to a natural state, and that this transition is both necessary and sufficient for pleasure.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that pleasure can occur without any preceding unnatural state (e.g., unexpected meeting) and transition can occur without pleasure (e.g., gradual recovery). Therefore, transition is neither necessary nor sufficient; pleasure requires perception and passivity.

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Sina reject Muhammad ibn Zakariyya's definition of pleasure as transition from an unnatural to a natural state?
A) Because transition is impossible without an intermediate state.
B) Because pleasure can occur without prior pain or unnatural state, and transition can occur without pleasure.
C) Because the definition only applies to physical pleasure, not intellectual.
D) Because the definition was not accepted by Galen.

Key Takeaway
Pain and pleasure are not mere perceptions or transitions; they require passivity (infiʿāl) and perception together, and conflating accidental change with essence is a common error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you felt deep pleasure from something you had not expected or longed for—like an unexpected kindness or a sudden glimpse of beauty. Did you attribute that pleasure to a mere change in circumstances, or did you recognize it as a gift requiring your active perception? How might this lesson correct your tendency to reduce spiritual joys to mere transitions?
?
When you experience pain, do you automatically assume it is because something harmful is present? This lesson shows that some harmful things (like moist imbalance) are not painful. How does this challenge your assumption that pain always follows harm? How should this reshape your trust in Allah's wisdom even when you do not feel the immediate harm of a situation?
?
If pleasure and pain require passivity (being affected), what does that imply about your own receptivity to divine experiences? Are you so preoccupied with change and sensation that you miss the essential need for a passive heart that truly perceives? What concrete habit can you adopt to cultivate a deeper passivity before the realities of faith?

Division of Pleasure and Pain: Sensory vs. Intellectual
The author divides pleasure and pain into sensory and intellectual and argues for the superiority of the intellectual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between sensory and intellectual pleasure and pain, and why intellectual pleasure is considered superior in quantity and quality.

Core Idea
Intellectual pleasure is greater—almost infinite in scope and deeper in perception—than sensory pleasure.

In this passage, the author builds on the earlier discussion of psychic qualities by classifying pleasure and pain. He argues that both come in two types—sensory and intellectual—and that the intellectual is superior. Before reading the source, consider why a spiritual pleasure might be considered more powerful than physical pleasure; the author provides a rational argument rooted in the nature of the intellect and its objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first a classification, then examples, then a comparison.
2
Identify the two divisions and their definitions
Find where the author defines sensory and intellectual pleasure/pain. Note the examples given for sensory pleasure.
3
Understand the argument for superiority
Pay attention to the two reasons: quantity (number of intelligibles) and quality (depth of perception). Ask yourself how this connects to the concept of 'acquired intellect'.
The author uses 'العقل المستفاد' (acquired intellect) as the culmination of intellectual perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ثم كل من اللذة والألم ينقسم إلى الحسي والعقلي حسب الإدراك، والعقلي أقوى لأن المعقولات أكثر وإدراك العقلي أكمل وكلاهما من الكيفيات النفسانية لأن المحسوس في الحسي هو ما يلتذ به، أو يتألم لا نفس اللذة أو الألم. فإنه ينقسم إليهما فينحصر فيهما عند أرباب البحث، أما الحسي فظاهر كتكيف العضو الذائق بالحلاوة والقوة الغضبية بتصور غلبة ما، والوهم بصورة شيء يرجوه إلى غير ذلك. وأما العقلي فلأن للجوهر العاقل أيضاً كمالاً وهو أن يتمثل فيه ما يتعقله من الواجب تعالى بقدر الاستطاعة ثم ما يتعقله من صور معلولاته المترتبة أعني الوجود كله تمثلاً مطابقاً خالياً عن شوائب الظنون والأوهام بحيث عقلاً مستفاداً على الإطلاق ولا شك أن هذا الكمال خير بالقياس إليه وأنه مدرك لهذا الكمال، ولحصول هذا الكمال له فإذن هو ملتذ بذلك وهذه هي اللذة العقلية، وأما الألم فهو أن يحصل له ضد هذا الكمال، ويدرك حصوله من حيث هو ضد ثم إذا قايسنا بين اللذتين فالعقلية أكثر كمية، وأقوى كيفية أما الأول : فلأن عدد تفاصيل المعقولات أكثر بل يكاد لا يتناهى . وأما الثاني : فلأن العقل يصل إلى كنه المعقول، والحس لا يدرك إلا ما يتعلق بظواهر الأجسام فتكون الكمالات العقلية أكثر وإدراكاتها أتم، فكذا
He said: Then each of pleasure and pain is divided into sensory and intellectual according to perception, and the intellectual is stronger because the intelligibles are more numerous and the perception of the intellectual is more perfect, and both are among the psychic qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya), because the perceived in the sensory is that which is enjoyed or suffered, not the essence of pleasure or pain. For it is divided into them and is confined to them according to the investigators. As for the sensory, it is apparent, like the qualification (takayyuf) of the tasting organ with sweetness, and the irascible faculty (al-quwwa al-ghaḍabiyya) by imagining a certain victory, and the estimative faculty (al-wahm) by an image of something hoped for, etc. As for the intellectual, because the rational substance (al-jawhar al-ʿāqil) also has a perfection (kamāl), which is that what it intelligizes (yataʿaqqalu) of the Necessary Exalted (al-wājib taʿālā) to the extent of capacity, then what it intelligizes of the forms of His arranged effects, i.e. the whole existence, is represented in it as a corresponding representation free from the impurities of conjectures and imaginations, such that it becomes an acquired intellect (ʿaqlan mustafādan) absolutely. And there is no doubt that this perfection is good in relation to it, and it perceives this perfection, and upon the attainment of this perfection for it, then it takes pleasure (multadhdh) therein, and this is intellectual pleasure. As for pain, it is that the opposite of this perfection obtains for it, and it perceives its attainment insofar as it is opposite. Then if we compare the two pleasures, the intellectual is greater in quantity and stronger in quality. As for the first, because the number of details of intelligibles is greater, indeed almost infinite. As for the second, because the intellect reaches the essence (kunn) of the intelligible, while sense perceives only what pertains to the appearances of bodies, so the intellectual perfections are more numerous and their perceptions more complete. Likewise.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a classification and comparison of pleasure and pain. The author states that both are divided into sensory and intellectual according to the type of perception. Intellectual pleasure is stronger (aqwā) because its objects (intelligibles) are more numerous and its perception is more perfect. Both belong to psychic qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya), since in sensory pleasure/pain, what is perceived is the object enjoyed or suffered, not the pleasure/pain itself. Then he clarifies that this division is exhaustive among researchers. Sensory examples include: the taste organ being qualified by sweetness, the irascible faculty by imagining a victory, and the estimative faculty by an image of something hoped for. As for intellectual pleasure, the rational substance (al-jawhar al-ʿāqil) has a perfection: representing in itself what it intelligizes of the Necessary Existent (God) to the extent possible, and then the forms of His effects (the whole cosmos) in a representation that accurately corresponds and is free from impurities of opinion and imagination, resulting in an acquired intellect (ʿaql mustafād). This perfection is good for it, and upon attaining it, it takes pleasure—this is intellectual pleasure. Conversely, intellectual pain occurs when the opposite of this perfection obtains and is perceived as such. The author then compares the two pleasures: intellectual is greater in quantity (because intelligibles are almost infinite) and stronger in quality (because intellect reaches the essence of the intelligible, while sense only perceives surface appearances). Therefore, intellectual perfections are more numerous and their perceptions more complete. The passage ends abruptly with 'فكذا' (likewise), implying a concluding remark about the superiority of intellectual pleasure.

Key Terms
الحسي (al-ḥissī)
العقلي (al-ʿaqlī)
الكيفيات النفسانية (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya)
الجوهر العاقل (al-jawhar al-ʿāqil)
العقل المستفاد (al-ʿaql al-mustafād)
كنه المعقول (kunn al-maʿqūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellectual pleasure is merely metaphorical or not a genuine felt experience.
Correction: The author treats intellectual pleasure as a real psychic quality (kayfiyya nafsiyya) that occurs when the rational substance attains its perfection of representing intelligibles. It is not metaphorical but a genuine form of delight.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is intellectual pleasure greater in quantity than sensory pleasure?
A) Because the intellect can perceive all sensory pleasures simultaneously.
B) Because the number of intelligibles is almost infinite.
C) Because intellectual pleasure lasts longer.
D) Because the intellect is unaffected by physical limitations.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual pleasure, grounded in the rational substance's perfection of representing intelligibles, surpasses sensory pleasure in both scope and depth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek pleasure, do you primarily chase sensory experiences or do you strive for intellectual and spiritual joys? Reflect on a recent decision: which type of pleasure motivated you? What does this reveal about your priorities?
?
The author argues that intellectual pleasure reaches the essence of things. Are you satisfied with superficial knowledge, or are you pursuing deep understanding of God and His creation? What is one step you can take today to deepen your intellectual and spiritual pleasure?
?
Intellectual pain is the opposite of perfection. When was the last time you felt pain because of ignorance or distance from truth? Did it move you to seek knowledge? Or did you numb it with sensory distractions? How will you respond next time?

Objection and Answer on Nisba in the Concept of Angle
Objection that nisba is part of the concept of angle and its rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians respond to objections against definitions by distinguishing between real and nominal definitions.

Core Idea
Not all definitions are created equal – the type of definition matters for objections.

This passage presents a classic objection-and-answer structure. The objector argues that if angle is a quality, then it cannot include relation (nisba). The answer cleverly deflects this by denying the minor premise and highlighting that the definition given is not a real definition. This shows the precision of argumentation in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the objection presents two premises: (1) relation is in the concept of angle, (2) no quality includes relation.
The objector's conclusion is implied: angle is not a quality.
2
Understand the answer's structure
The answer has two parts: first, deny the minor premise (the claim that no quality includes relation). Second, note that the objection would only be valid if the definition were a real definition.
This shows that the definition might be nominal or descriptive, not real.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does the type of definition matter? How does this distinction protect the original definition from being refuted?
This connects to the learning objective about definition types in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : النسبة مأخوذة في مفهومه ولا شيء من الكيف كذلك . أجيب بمنع الصغرى، وإنما يتم لو كان المذكور في تعريفه حداً حقيقياً له .
For it is said: relation (nisba) is taken in its concept, and nothing of quality (kayf) is so. It was answered by denying the minor premise, and it would only be valid if what is mentioned in its definition is a real definition (ḥadd ḥaqīqī) of it.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of angle. The objector claims that 'relation' (nisba) is included in the concept of angle, but no quality includes relation. The answer counters by denying the minor premise (that no quality includes relation). It adds that the objection would only hold if the given definition were a real definition of angle, implying that the definition is not real but nominal or descriptive.

Key Terms
نسبة
كيف
صغرى
حد حقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Objections to a definition automatically refute it if they point out a logical inconsistency.
Correction: The answer shows that objections must consider the type of definition; a nominal definition can include relation even if the category of quality typically does not.

Checkpoint
What is the key move in the answer to the objection?
A) Affirming the minor premise
B) Denying the minor premise and noting the definition type
C) Redefining angle
D) Accepting the objection

Key Takeaway
The validity of an objection to a definition depends on whether the definition is real or nominal, and its premises must be checked.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Defending the Definition of Shape
Objection that the definition of shape only includes bodily shapes, and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of shape (shakl) is defended against the objection that it only covers bodily forms, by clarifying the term 'body' as mathematical body.

Core Idea
A definition's precision depends on correctly interpreting its key terms.

In this passage, we encounter a classic objection–answer exchange. The objector claims that the definition of shape mistakenly excludes surface shapes. The defender clarifies that 'body' here means the mathematical body, which is the substrate for surfaces. This defense shows how careful terminology safeguards a definition's scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the objection: the definition allegedly covers only bodily shapes, not surface shapes.
Notice the directness of the objection: 'إنما يتناول الأشكال الجسمية دون السطحية'.
2
Analyze the answer
The answer reinterprets 'body' as 'mathematical body' (al-jism al-taʿlīmī). Understand why this solves the objection: because the mathematical body is the substrate for surface boundaries.
The answer also explains why 'body' was preferred over 'line' or 'surface'.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why the mathematical body can be imagined 'with the condition of nothing else', while line and surface require further abstraction. This supports the definition's inclusiveness.
This highlights the subtlety in kalam definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على تعريفه بأنه إنما يتناول الأشكال الجسمية دون السطحية . وأجيب بأن المراد بالجسم ههنا هو التعليمي لأنه بالذات معروض الحدود السطحية كما أن السطح معروض الحدود الخطية، وإنما خص التعليمي بالذكر دون الخط والسطح لأنه الذي يمكن تخيله بشرط لا شيء بخلافهما.
And it was objected to his definition that it only covers bodily shapes (ashkāl jismiyya) and not surface shapes. It was answered that what is meant by 'body' here is the mathematical body (al-taʿlīmī), because it is per se the substrate of surface boundaries just as the surface is the substrate of linear boundaries, and he only specified the mathematical body to the exclusion of line and surface because it can be imagined with the condition of nothing else besides them.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage records an objection to the definition of shape and its answer. The objector says the definition only applies to bodily shapes (three-dimensional), not surface shapes (two-dimensional). The answer clarifies that the term 'body' in the definition refers to the mathematical body (al-jism al-taʿlīmī), which is the substrate for surface boundaries, just as surfaces are substrates for linear boundaries. The reason for specifying 'body' rather than line or surface is that it can be imagined without any other condition, unlike line and surface which require further abstraction.

Key Terms
شكل
جسم
تعليمي
سطح
حدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of shape excludes surface shapes (like squares and circles).
Correction: The definition includes surface shapes because 'body' refers to the mathematical body, which directly underlies surface boundaries.

Checkpoint
Why does the author specify 'mathematical body' in the definition of shape?
A) To exclude surface shapes.
B) Because it is the substrate of surface boundaries and can be imagined without additional conditions.
C) To limit the definition to three-dimensional objects.
D) Because it is the only type of body that exists.

Key Takeaway
The definition of shape is inclusive of surface shapes because 'body' refers to the mathematical body that underlies surfaces.

For example, in your understanding of worship, actions, or the Names of Allah. Ask Allah to grant you the sincerity to seek the intended meaning beyond the surface.

The Definition of Shape as Configuration of Boundaries
Definition of shape with two cases and a note of hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical kalam definition of shape (shakl) as the configuration of boundaries around a surface or body, and the scholarly hesitation about what quantity is primary.

Core Idea
Shape is not the thing itself but the arrangement of its boundaries.

This lesson presents a precise definition of shape (shakl) from the kalam tradition. The passage clarifies that shape is not the surface or body itself but the configuration of its enclosing boundaries. It also introduces a subtle point of hesitation regarding which quantity is the primary subject of shape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's meaning.
Notice the phrase 'التحقيق أن' indicating the verified view.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage gives two scenarios: boundaries around a surface (lines) and boundaries around a body (surfaces). Identify these and note how the boundaries differ.
3
Consider the hesitation
The author mentions a hesitation (تردد) about whether the quantity subject to shape is the boundaries, the enclosed surface, or the enclosed body. Reflect on why this distinction might matter.
This hesitation shows that the definition involves a subtle priority question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالتحقيق أن الشكل هيئة إحاطة الحد أو الحدود بالسطح أو الجسم. والحدود على الأول خطوط، وعلى الثاني سطوح، والكمية المعروضة بالذات للشكل هو الحدود المحيطة أم السطح أم الجسم المحاط فيه تردد.
The verified view is that shape (shakl) is the configuration (hay'a) of the boundary (ḥadd) or boundaries surrounding the surface or body. The boundaries in the first case are lines, and in the second are surfaces. And there is hesitation whether the quantity (kammiyya) that is per se the subject of shape is the surrounding boundaries or the surface or the body that is enclosed.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage gives the correct definition of shape: it is the configuration (hay'a) of a boundary or boundaries that enclose either a surface (two-dimensional) or a body (three-dimensional). For a surface, the boundaries are lines; for a body, the boundaries are surfaces. The author then notes a point of scholarly hesitation: what is the quantity that is the primary subject of shape—is it the boundaries themselves, the enclosed surface, or the enclosed body? This is not settled here.

Key Terms

هيئة
إحاطة
حد
سطح
جسم
كمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think shape is the enclosed surface or body itself.
Correction: Shape is defined as the configuration of boundaries, not the enclosed entity.

Checkpoint

A) The enclosed surface or body.
B) The configuration of boundaries surrounding a surface or body.
C) The boundaries themselves.
D) The quantity of the surface.

Key Takeaway
Shape (shakl) is defined as the configuration of boundaries enclosing a surface or body, not the enclosed entity itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Engineers' Usage of Angle and Its Types
Explanation of engineers' synecdochic use and definition of plane and solid angle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how engineers use the term 'angle' to refer to the thing having the angle, and the difference between plane and solid angles.

Core Idea
Angle often means the thing that has the angle, not an abstract geometric entity.

This passage from a classical work on kalam clarifies a common usage in geometry: when engineers talk about an angle being a surface or divisible, they are using 'angle' synecdochically to mean the object that has the angle. It then defines two fundamental types of angles: plane and solid. Understanding this terminological nuance is important for reading geometric texts and their application in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Pay attention to the flow: first the explanation of engineers' usage, then Euclid's definition, then the two types.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the synecdochic usage
Note that when engineers say 'the angle is a surface', they mean the thing that has the angle (dhū al-zāwiya). Similarly, 'shape' is used for the thing shaped. This is a key interpretive point.
Synecdoche: part for the whole.
3
Distinguish plane and solid angles
Euclid's definition of angle as the contact of two lines refers to the configuration (hay'a) at the contact—this is the plane angle. The solid angle involves two surfaces meeting at a line.
Solid angle is three-dimensional.
4
Apply the understanding
Consider why this distinction matters: in theological arguments about creation and attributes, geometric analogies are used. Knowing the precise meaning prevents misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يقع في عبارات المهندسين من كونها سطحاً وقابلاً للتجزي والمساواة والمقاومة بالذات فميني على أنهم يريدون بالزاوية ذا الزاوية كما يريدون بالشكل المشكل فيقولون المثلث شكل تحيط به ثلاثة أضلاع وما ذكر اقليدس من أن الزاوية تماس الخطين فمعناه الهيئة الحاصلة عند تماسهما هذا هو الزاوية المسطحة . وأما المجسمة فهي جسم يحيط به سطحان يلتقيان بخط أو الهيئة الحاصلة عند ذلك.
And what occurs in the expressions of the engineers (muhandisīn) that it is a surface and divisible, equalable, and resistible per se is based on that they mean by angle the thing having the angle (dhū al-zāwiya), just as they mean by shape the thing shaped (al-mushakkal), so they say: 'The triangle is a shape surrounded by three sides.' And what Euclid mentioned that the angle is the contact of two lines means the configuration (hay'a) that arises at their contact. This is the plane angle (al-zāwiya al-musaṭṭaḥa). As for the solid angle (al-mujassama), it is a body surrounded by two surfaces meeting at a line, or the configuration that arises at that.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage clarifies the terminology used by geometricians (engineers). When they say that an angle is a surface and is divisible, equal, and resistible per se, they are using 'angle' to mean 'the thing that has the angle' (dhū al-zāwiya), analogous to when they say 'the triangle is a shape'—they mean the triangle itself, not just the shape. The passage also distinguishes between two types of angle: the plane angle (zāwiya musaṭṭaḥa), which is the configuration at the meeting of two lines on a plane, and the solid angle (zāwiya mujassama), which occurs when two surfaces meet at a line, producing a three-dimensional corner, and it can be defined as either the resulting body or the configuration at that meeting.

Key Terms
زاوية
مهندس
شكل
مشكل
اقليدس
تماس
زاوية مسطحة
زاوية مجسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think geometric statements about angles being surfaces or divisible are literal definitions.
Correction: The author clarifies that these are synecdochic usages: 'angle' refers to the object having the angle, not the abstract angle itself.

Checkpoint
When engineers say 'angle is a surface,' what do they actually mean?
A) The angle itself is a surface.
B) They mean the thing that has the angle.
C) They are using a different definition.
D) They are mistaken.

Key Takeaway
Engineers use 'angle' synecdochically to mean the object that has the angle, and angles are classified as plane (configuration of two lines) or solid (configuration of two surfaces meeting at a line).

Opening of the Fourth Chapter on Al-Ayn: Two Investigations
Al-Taftazani opens the fourth chapter with two investigatory headings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the fourth chapter of Sharh al-Maqasid, on the topic of 'where' (al-ayn), opens with two distinct investigations: one on the necessary existence of the universe and one on the motion of interior parts of a moving body.

Core Idea
The fourth chapter begins with two foundational claims about place and motion.

Al-Taftazani structures his discussion of 'al-ayn' (where) by first presenting two key propositions that will be argued in detail. This lesson introduces those propositions and sets the stage for the detailed proofs that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening phrase
Read the Arabic phrase 'الفصل الرابع في الأين وفيه مبحثان' to see how the chapter is introduced. Notice that it explicitly states the chapter has two investigations.
The word 'مبحثان' indicates two sections of inquiry.
2
Identify the two investigations
Find the two numbered items: 1) 'الكون وجوده ضروري' and 2) 'الباطن من أجزاء الجسم المتحرك متحرك'. These are the headings for the investigations.
They are concise statements that will be expanded later.
3
Reflect on their role
Consider why these two claims are placed at the beginning. They likely serve as foundational premises for the rest of the chapter's discussion on place and motion.
This prepares you for the detailed arguments that follow.

The second investigation is 'the interior of the parts of a moving body is moving' — a statement about the nature of motion and its internal parts. These are preliminary propositions that will be elaborated in the subsequent sections of the chapter.

Reality of al-Ayn and the Four Types of al-Kawn
Defines al-ayn and classifies al-kawn as necessary with four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ayn (whereness) as spatial occurrence, its necessary existence, and the classification of its four types based on the relation between two atoms in place.

Core Idea
Spatial relations between atoms divide into separation and conjunction, each with subtypes.

This lesson introduces a foundational concept in kalam cosmology: the reality of 'whereness' (al-ayn). The author defines al-ayn as spatial occurrence (al-kawn) in place, then establishes that its existence is necessary. The core of the passage is a classification of spatial relations into four types based on whether a third atom can intervene between two atoms. Understanding this classification is essential for later discussions on atomism and accident theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the technical terms: al-ayn, al-kawn, jawhar, hayyiz, iftiraq, ijtima'.
The passage is concise but packs three claims: definition, necessity, classification.
2
Identify the definition of al-ayn
The passage defines al-ayn as 'al-kawn in place' (al-kawn fi al-hayyiz). Understand that here 'kawn' means spatial occurrence, not existence.
This is a technical definition specific to this treatise.
3
Understand the necessity of al-kawn
The author states that the existence of al-kawn is necessary (wujuduhu daruri). Grasp why: every atom must be in some place, so spatial occurrence is a necessary attribute.
4
Analyze the classification of four types
The classification hinges on the relation between two atoms. If a third can fit between them, it's separation (iftiraq) with degrees; if not, it's conjunction (ijtima'). The terms mujawara and mumassa are alternative names for the nearest forms.
Mumassa is the closest form of conjunction (contiguity).
5
Apply the classification
Draw a simple diagram: two atoms and a space between them. Determine if a third atom can fit. This yields the four types: distant separation, close separation, conjunction (adjacency), and contiguity.
Think of atoms as points; the types are determined by distance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقيقة الأين قال الفصل الرابع في الأين وهو الكون في [الحيز] وسلوكه على طريقين: الأول للمتكلمين وهو مبحثان: المبحث الأول: الكون وجوده ضروري وأنواعه أربعة لأن حصول الجوهر في الحيز إن اعتبر بالنسبة إلى جوهر آخر فإن أمكن تخلل ثالث بينهما فافتراق على تفاوت أقسامه في القرب والبعد وإلا فاجتماع ومن أسمائه المجاورة والمماسة على الأقرب.
The reality of al-ayn (whereness). He said: The fourth chapter on al-ayn. And it is al-kawn (spatial occurrence) in place and its pursuit is on two paths: the first for the mutakallimun, and it has two investigations: the first investigation: al-kawn, its existence is necessary and its types are four, because the occurrence of the jawhar (atom) in place, if considered in relation to another jawhar: if a third can be inserted between them, then it is iftiraq (separation) according to its varying degrees in proximity and distance, and otherwise it is ijtima' (conjunction). Among its names are al-mujawara (adjacency) and al-mumassa (contiguity) on the nearest view.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage defines the reality of al-ayn (whereness) and begins the fourth chapter on the subject. It introduces al-kawn (spatial occurrence) as the context and states that its investigation proceeds along two paths: the first is that of the mutakallimun (theologians), which has two sub-investigations. The text then presents the first investigation: al-kawn's existence is necessary (wujūduhū ḍarūrī), and its types are four. This classification stems from considering a jawhar (atom or indivisible substance) situated in a place (ḥayyiz) in relation to another jawhar. If a third jawhar can fit between them, the relation is iftiraq (separation), which admits degrees of proximity and distance; if no third can intervene, the relation is ijtima' (conjunction). The passage notes that these spatial relations are also referred to as mujawara (adjacency) and mumassa (contiguity), with the latter being the closest form. The passage thus establishes the foundational categories of spatial relations for subsequent theological discussion.

Key Terms
أين (al-ayn: whereness)
كون (al-kawn: spatial occurrence)
جوهر (jawhar: atom/substance)
حيز (ḥayyiz: place)
افتراق (iftiraq: separation)
اجتماع (ijtima': conjunction)
مجاورة (mujawara: adjacency)
مماسة (mumassa: contiguity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-kawn means existence in general, not specifically spatial occurrence.
Correction: In this context, al-kawn is a technical term meaning 'spatial occurrence' or 'being in a place', not existence as such.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Al-kawn is a necessary attribute of atoms, and its types are determined by the possibility of a third atom intervening, yielding separation or conjunction with degrees.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are an atom (jawhar) in this creation. Your spatial relation to others is a necessary accident. Reflect: Are you in ijtima' (conjunction) with goodness, truth, and righteous company, or are you in iftiraq (separation) due to sin or neglect? What one change will you make today to draw nearer to what is truly adjacent to Allah's pleasure?
?
The passage teaches that al-kawn is necessary for every atom. Your existence in place is a sign of your utter dependence on Allah for every moment. How does this awareness of your own necessity and contingency affect your sense of humility and reliance on Allah?
?
Consider the degrees of proximity in separation. In your relationships with family or community, are there gaps that could be bridged? What step can you take to move from distant iftiraq to closer mujawara or even mumassa in love and good deeds?
